<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Benjamin Franklin</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Benjamin Franklin</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a> <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Fellow of the Royal Society of Edinburgh">FRSE</a></span> (January 17, 1706 [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> January 6, 1705]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engber_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engber-1">[1]</a></sup> –  April 17, 1790) was an American <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> and one of the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a>. Franklin was a leading author, printer, <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_philosophers" title="List of political philosophers">political theorist</a>, politician, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freemason" title="Freemason">freemason</a>, postmaster, scientist, inventor, humorist, civic activist, statesman, and diplomat. As a scientist, he was a major figure in the <a href="/wiki/American_Enlightenment" title="American Enlightenment">American Enlightenment</a> and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_physics" title="History of physics">history of physics</a> for his discoveries and theories regarding electricity. As an inventor, he is known for the <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning rod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bifocals" title="Bifocals">bifocals</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_stove" title="Franklin stove">Franklin stove</a>, among other inventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> He founded many civic organizations, including the <a href="/wiki/Library_Company_of_Philadelphia" title="Library Company of Philadelphia">Library Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>'s first <a href="/wiki/Union_Fire_Company" title="Union Fire Company">fire department</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin earned the title of "The First American" for his early and indefatigable campaigning for <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">colonial unity</a>, initially as an author and spokesman in London for several colonies. As the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_France" title="United States Ambassador to France">United States Ambassador to France</a>, he exemplified the emerging American nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Franklin was foundational in defining the American ethos as a marriage of the practical values of thrift, hard work, education, community spirit, self-governing institutions, and opposition to authoritarianism both political and religious, with the scientific and tolerant values of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>. In the words of historian <a href="/wiki/Henry_Steele_Commager" title="Henry Steele Commager">Henry Steele Commager</a>, "In a Franklin could be merged the virtues of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritanism" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a> without its defects, the illumination of the Enlightenment without its heat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> To <a href="/wiki/Walter_Isaacson" title="Walter Isaacson">Walter Isaacson</a>, this makes Franklin "the most accomplished American of his age and the most influential in inventing the type of society America would become."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin became a successful newspaper editor and printer in Philadelphia, the leading city in the colonies, publishing the <i><a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Gazette" title="Pennsylvania Gazette">Pennsylvania Gazette</a></i> at the age of 23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> He became wealthy publishing this and <i><a href="/wiki/Poor_Richard%27s_Almanack" title="Poor Richard's Almanack">Poor Richard's Almanack</a></i>, which he authored under the <a href="/wiki/Pseudonym" title="Pseudonym">pseudonym</a> "Richard Saunders". After 1767, he was associated with the <i><a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Chronicle" title="Pennsylvania Chronicle">Pennsylvania Chronicle</a></i>, a newspaper that was known for its revolutionary sentiments and criticisms of British policies.
</p><p>He pioneered and was first president of <a href="/wiki/Academy_and_College_of_Philadelphia" title="Academy and College of Philadelphia">Academy and College of Philadelphia</a> which opened in 1751 and later became the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>. He organized and was the first secretary of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> and was elected president in 1769. Franklin became a national hero in America as an agent for several colonies when he spearheaded an effort in London to have the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament of Great Britain</a> repeal the unpopular <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act_1765" title="Stamp Act 1765">Stamp Act</a>. An accomplished diplomat, he was widely admired among the French as American minister to Paris and was a major figure in the development of positive <a href="/wiki/France%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="France–United States relations">Franco-American relations</a>. His efforts proved vital for the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> in securing shipments of crucial munitions from France.
</p><p>He was promoted to deputy postmaster-general for the British colonies in 1753, having been Philadelphia postmaster for many years, and this enabled him to set up the first national communications network. During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">revolution</a>, he became the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">United States Postmaster General</a>. He was active in community affairs and colonial and state politics, as well as national and international affairs. From 1785 to 1788, he served as <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Executive_Council_of_the_Commonwealth_of_Pennsylvania#Presidents_of_Council" title="Supreme Executive Council of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania">governor of Pennsylvania</a>. He initially owned and dealt in slaves but, by the 1750s, he argued against <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> from an economic perspective and became one of the most prominent <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionists</a>.
</p><p>His colorful life and legacy of scientific and political achievement, and his status as one of America's most influential Founding Fathers, have seen Franklin honored more than two centuries after his death on <a href="/wiki/Franklin_half_dollar" title="Franklin half dollar">coinage</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_one_hundred-dollar_bill" title="United States one hundred-dollar bill">$100 bill</a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Benjamin_Franklin_(SSBN-640)" title="USS Benjamin Franklin (SSBN-640)">warships</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_named_for_Benjamin_Franklin" title="List of places named for Benjamin Franklin">the names of many towns</a>, counties, educational institutions, and corporations, as well as countless <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_in_popular_culture" title="Benjamin Franklin in popular culture">cultural references</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestry">Ancestry</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternal">Maternal</span></h3>
<p>Benjamin's mother, Abiah Folger, was born in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nantucket,_Massachusetts" title="Nantucket, Massachusetts">Nantucket, Massachusetts</a>, on August 15, 1667, to <a href="/wiki/Peter_Folger_(Nantucket_settler)" title="Peter Folger (Nantucket settler)">Peter Folger</a>, a miller and schoolteacher, and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Morrell_Folger" title="Mary Morrell Folger">Mary Morrell Folger</a>, a former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" title="Indentured servant">indentured servant</a>.
She came from a Puritan family that was among the first Pilgrims to flee to <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> for <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">religious freedom</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">King Charles I of England</a> began persecuting Puritans. They sailed for Boston in 1635.
</p><p>Her father was "the sort of rebel destined to transform colonial America."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Court_clerk" title="Court clerk">clerk of the court</a>, he was jailed for disobeying the local magistrate in defense of middle-class shopkeepers and artisans in conflict with wealthy landowners. Ben Franklin followed in his grandfather's footsteps in his battles against the wealthy <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">Penn</a> family that owned the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pennsylvania" title="Province of Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania Colony</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paternal">Paternal</span></h3>
<p>Benjamin Franklin's father, <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Franklin" title="Josiah Franklin">Josiah Franklin</a>, was a <a href="/wiki/Tallow" title="Tallow">tallow</a> chandler, a soap-maker and a candle-maker. Josiah was born at <a href="/wiki/Ecton,_Northamptonshire" title="Ecton, Northamptonshire">Ecton, Northamptonshire</a>, England on December 23, 1657, the son of Thomas Franklin, a blacksmith-farmer, and Jane White.
</p><p>Benjamin's father and all four of his grandparents were born in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h3>
<p>Josiah had seventeen children with his two wives. He married his first wife, Anne Child, in about 1677 in Ecton and immigrated with her to <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> in 1683; they had three children before immigrating, and four after. Following her death, Josiah was married to Abiah Folger on July 9, 1689 in the <a href="/wiki/Old_South_Meeting_House" title="Old South Meeting House">Old South Meeting House</a> by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Willard" title="Samuel Willard">Samuel Willard</a>. Benjamin, their eighth child, was Josiah Franklin's fifteenth child and tenth and last son.
</p>
<div class="noresize"><table class="collapsible collapsed" style="margin:0.3em auto;clear:none;min-width:60em;width:auto;font-size:88%;border:1px solid #aaa"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.3em 0.2em 4.3em;background:none;width:auto">Ancestors of Benjamin Franklin</th></tr><tr><td style="text-align:center"><table style="border-collapse:separate;border-spacing:0;line-height:130%;margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><tbody><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td colspan="2" rowspan="28"> </td><td rowspan="13"> </td><td colspan="2" rowspan="12"> </td><td rowspan="5"> </td><td colspan="2" rowspan="4"> </td><td> </td><td colspan="4" rowspan="2" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #bfc;">8. Abiah Folger<br/>b. August 15, 1667, <a href="/wiki/Nantucket" title="Nantucket">Nantucket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a></td><td colspan="7" rowspan="2"> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td rowspan="9"> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td colspan="2" rowspan="8"> </td><td rowspan="7" style="border-bottom:#000 solid 1px; border-left:#000 solid 1px;"> </td><td colspan="2" rowspan="6"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td colspan="4" rowspan="2"> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td colspan="4" rowspan="2"> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td colspan="4" rowspan="2"> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td colspan="4" rowspan="2" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #ffc;">7. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_Morrill" title="Mary Morrill">Mary Morrill</a><br/>b. c. 1619, England</td><td colspan="3" rowspan="2"> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td> </td></tr><tr style="text-align:center"><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td><td> </td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_in_Boston">Early life in Boston</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="719" data-file-width="620" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG/170px-Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG/255px-Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG/340px-Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace_2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin's birthplace on <a href="/wiki/Milk_Street,_Boston" title="Milk Street, Boston">Milk Street</a>, Boston, Massachusetts</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="1728" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg/340px-Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_Birthplace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin's birthplace site directly across from <a href="/wiki/Old_South_Meeting_House" title="Old South Meeting House">Old South Meeting House</a> on <a href="/wiki/Milk_Street,_Boston" title="Milk Street, Boston">Milk Street</a> is commemorated by a <a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">bust</a> above the second floor facade of this building.</div></div></div>
<p>Benjamin Franklin was born on <a href="/wiki/Milk_Street,_Boston" title="Milk Street, Boston">Milk Street</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Massachusetts_Bay" title="Province of Massachusetts Bay">Massachusetts</a>, on January 17, 1706,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engber_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Engber-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calendar_group=Note_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-calendar_group=Note-12">[12]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">baptized</a> at Old South Meeting House. He was one of seventeen children born to <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Franklin" title="Josiah Franklin">Josiah Franklin</a>, and one of ten born by Josiah's second wife, Abiah Folger; the daughter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Foulger" title="Peter Foulger">Peter Foulger</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_Morrill" title="Mary Morrill">Mary Morrill</a>. Among Benjamin's siblings were his older brother <a href="/wiki/James_Franklin_(printer)" title="James Franklin (printer)">James</a> and his younger sister <a href="/wiki/Jane_Mecom" title="Jane Mecom">Jane</a>.
</p><p>Josiah wanted Ben to attend school with the clergy, but only had enough money to send him to school for two years. He attended <a href="/wiki/Boston_Latin_School" title="Boston Latin School">Boston Latin School</a> but did not graduate; he continued his education through voracious reading. Although "his parents talked of the church as a career"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autobio_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-autobio-13">[13]</a></sup> for Franklin, his schooling ended when he was ten. He worked for his father for a time, and at 12 he became an <a href="/wiki/Apprenticeship" title="Apprenticeship">apprentice</a> to his brother James, a printer, who taught Ben the printing trade. When Ben was 15, James founded <i><a href="/wiki/The_New-England_Courant" title="The New-England Courant">The New-England Courant</a></i>, which was <a href="/wiki/History_of_American_newspapers" title="History of American newspapers">the first truly independent newspaper in the colonies</a>.
</p><p>When denied the chance to write a letter to the paper for publication, Franklin adopted the pseudonym of "<a href="/wiki/Silence_Dogood" title="Silence Dogood">Silence Dogood</a>", a middle-aged widow. Mrs. Dogood's letters were published, and became a subject of conversation around town. Neither James nor the <i>Courant'</i>s readers were aware of the ruse, and James was unhappy with Ben when he discovered the popular correspondent was his younger brother. Franklin was an advocate of free speech from an early age. When his brother was jailed for three weeks in 1722 for publishing material unflattering to the governor, young Franklin took over the newspaper and had Mrs. Dogood (quoting <i><a href="/wiki/Cato%27s_Letters" title="Cato's Letters">Cato's Letters</a></i>) proclaim: "Without freedom of thought there can be no such thing as wisdom and no such thing as public liberty without freedom of speech."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Franklin left his apprenticeship without his brother's permission, and in so doing became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fugitive_from_justice" title="Fugitive from justice">fugitive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_-_ita,_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="674" data-file-width="405" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Franklin_-_ita%2C_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg/170px-Franklin_-_ita%2C_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Franklin_-_ita%2C_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg/255px-Franklin_-_ita%2C_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Franklin_-_ita%2C_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg/340px-Franklin_-_ita%2C_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_-_ita,_1825_-_766672_R.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>La scuola della economia e della morale</i> (1825)</div></div></div>
<p>At age 17, Franklin ran away to Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, seeking a new start in a new city. When he first arrived, he worked in several printer shops around town, but he was not satisfied by the immediate prospects. After a few months, while working in a printing house, Franklin was convinced by Pennsylvania Governor <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Keith,_4th_Baronet" title="Sir William Keith, 4th Baronet">Sir William Keith</a> to go to London, ostensibly to acquire the equipment necessary for establishing another newspaper in Philadelphia. Finding Keith's promises of backing a newspaper empty, Franklin worked as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typesetter" title="Typesetter">typesetter</a> in a printer's shop in what is now the <a href="/wiki/St_Bartholomew-the-Great" title="St Bartholomew-the-Great">Church of St Bartholomew-the-Great</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Smithfield,_London" title="Smithfield, London">Smithfield</a> area of London. Following this, he returned to Philadelphia in 1726 with the help of Thomas Denham, a merchant who employed Franklin as clerk, shopkeeper, and bookkeeper in his business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Junto_and_library">Junto and library</span></h3>
<p>In 1727, Benjamin Franklin, then 21, created the <a href="/wiki/Junto_(club)" title="Junto (club)">Junto</a>, a group of "like minded aspiring artisans and tradesmen who hoped to improve themselves while they improved their community." The Junto was a discussion group for issues of the day; it subsequently gave rise to many organizations in Philadelphia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The Junto was modeled after English coffeehouses that Franklin knew well, and which had become the center of the spread of Enlightenment ideas in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reading was a great pastime of the Junto, but books were rare and expensive. The members created a library initially assembled from their own books after Franklin wrote:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A proposition was made by me that since our books were often referr'd to in our disquisitions upon the inquiries, it might be convenient for us to have them altogether where we met, that upon occasion they might be consulted; and by thus clubbing our books to a common library, we should, while we lik'd to keep them together, have each of us the advantage of using the books of all the other members, which would be nearly as beneficial as if each owned the whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>This did not suffice, however. Franklin conceived the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Subscription_library" title="Subscription library">subscription library</a>, which would pool the funds of the members to buy books for all to read. This was the birth of the <a href="/wiki/Library_Company_of_Philadelphia" title="Library Company of Philadelphia">Library Company of Philadelphia</a>: its charter was composed by Franklin in 1731. In 1732, Franklin hired the first American librarian, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Timothee" title="Louis Timothee">Louis Timothee</a>. The Library Company is now a great scholarly and <a href="/wiki/Library#Research_library" title="Library">research library</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newspaperman">Newspaperman</span></h3>
<p>Upon Denham's death, Franklin returned to his former trade. In 1728, Franklin had set up a printing house in partnership with <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Meredith" title="Hugh Meredith">Hugh Meredith</a>; the following year he became the publisher of a newspaper called <i><a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Gazette" title="Pennsylvania Gazette">The Pennsylvania Gazette</a></i>. The <i>Gazette</i> gave Franklin a forum for agitation about a variety of local reforms and initiatives through printed essays and observations. Over time, his commentary, and his adroit cultivation of a positive image as an industrious and intellectual young man, earned him a great deal of social respect. But even after Franklin had achieved fame as a scientist and statesman, he habitually signed his letters with the unpretentious 'B. Franklin, Printer.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_the_printer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2204" data-file-width="3836" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Franklin_the_printer.jpg/290px-Franklin_the_printer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Franklin_the_printer.jpg/435px-Franklin_the_printer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Franklin_the_printer.jpg/580px-Franklin_the_printer.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_the_printer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Benjamin Franklin (center) at work on a <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>. Reproduction of a Charles Mills painting by the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Publishing_Company" title="Detroit Publishing Company">Detroit Publishing Company</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1732, Ben Franklin published the first <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German-language</a> newspaper in America – <i>Die Philadelphische Zeitung</i> – although it failed after only one year, because four other newly founded German papers quickly dominated the newspaper market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Franklin printed Moravian religious books in German. Franklin often visited <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem,_Pennsylvania" title="Bethlehem, Pennsylvania">Bethlehem, Pennsylvania</a> staying at the <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Sun_Inn" title="Moravian Sun Inn">Moravian Sun Inn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In a 1751 pamphlet on demographic growth and its implications for the colonies, he called the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Dutch" title="Pennsylvania Dutch">Pennsylvania Germans</a> "Palatine Boors" who could never acquire the "Complexion" of the English settlers and referred to "Blacks and Tawneys" as weakening the social structure of the colonies. Although Franklin apparently reconsidered shortly thereafter, and the phrases were omitted from all later printings of the pamphlet, his views may have played a role in his political defeat in 1764.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin saw the printing press as a device to instruct colonial Americans in moral virtue. In <i>Benjamin Franklin's Journalism</i>, Ralph Frasca argues he saw this as a service to God, because he understood moral virtue in terms of actions, thus, doing good provides a service to God. Despite his own moral lapses, Franklin saw himself as uniquely qualified to instruct Americans in morality. He tried to influence American moral life through construction of a printing network based on a chain of partnerships from the Carolinas to New England. Franklin thereby invented the first newspaper chain. It was more than a business venture, for like many publishers since, he believed that the press had a public-service duty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="395" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg/170px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg/255px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg/340px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Benjamin_Franklin.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coat of Arms of Benjamin Franklin</div></div></div>
<p>When Franklin established himself in Philadelphia, shortly before 1730, the town boasted two "wretched little" news sheets, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Bradford" title="Andrew Bradford">Andrew Bradford</a>'s <i>The American Weekly Mercury</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Keimer" title="Samuel Keimer">Samuel Keimer</a>'s <i>Universal Instructor in all Arts and Sciences, and Pennsylvania Gazette</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This instruction in all arts and sciences consisted of weekly extracts from <i>Chambers's Universal Dictionary</i>. Franklin quickly did away with all this when he took over the <i>Instructor</i> and made it <i>The Pennsylvania Gazette</i>. The <i>Gazette</i> soon became Franklin's characteristic organ, which he freely used for satire, for the play of his wit, even for sheer excess of mischief or of fun. From the first, he had a way of adapting his models to his own uses. <span id="The_Busy-Body"></span><span id="The_Busybody"></span>The series of essays called "The Busy-Body", which he wrote for Bradford's <i>American Mercury</i> in 1729, followed the general <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Addison" title="Joseph Addison">Addisonian</a> form, already modified to suit homelier conditions. The thrifty Patience, in her busy little shop, complaining of the useless visitors who waste her valuable time, is related to the ladies who address Mr. Spectator. The Busy-Body himself is a true Censor Morum, as <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Bickerstaff" title="Isaac Bickerstaff">Isaac Bickerstaff</a> had been in the <i>Tatler</i>. And a number of the fictitious characters, Ridentius, Eugenius, Cato, and Cretico, represent traditional 18th-century classicism. Even this Franklin could use for contemporary satire, since Cretico, the "sowre Philosopher", is evidently a portrait of Franklin's rival, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Keimer" title="Samuel Keimer">Samuel Keimer</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Pennsylvania Gazette</i>, like most other newspapers of the period, was often poorly printed. Franklin was busy with a hundred matters outside of his printing office, and never seriously attempted to raise the mechanical standards of his trade. Nor did he ever properly edit or collate the chance medley of stale items that passed for news in the <i>Gazette.</i> His influence on the practical side of journalism was minimal.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> On the other hand, his advertisements of books show his very great interest in popularizing secular literature. Undoubtedly his paper contributed to the broader culture that distinguished Pennsylvania from her neighbors before the Revolution. Like many publishers, Franklin built up a book shop in his printing office; he took the opportunity to read new books before selling them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Franklin had mixed success in his plan to establish an inter-colonial network of newspapers that would produce a profit for him and disseminate virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> He began in <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a>, in 1731. After the second editor died, his widow Elizabeth Timothy took over and made it a success, 1738–46. She was one of the colonial era's first woman printers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> For three decades Franklin maintained a close business relationship with her and her son Peter who took over in 1746.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The <i>Gazette</i> had a policy of impartiality in political debates, while creating the opportunity for public debate, which encouraged others to challenge authority. Editor Peter Timothy avoided blandness and crude bias, and after 1765 increasingly took a patriotic stand in the growing crisis with Great Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> However, Franklin's <i>Connecticut Gazette</i> (1755–68) proved unsuccessful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freemason">Freemason</span></h3>
<p>In 1731, Franklin was initiated into the local <a href="/wiki/Masonic_lodge" title="Masonic lodge">Masonic lodge</a>. He became Grand Master in 1734, indicating his rapid rise to prominence in Pennsylvania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HC_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-HC-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> That same year, he edited and published the first Masonic book in the Americas, a reprint of <a href="/wiki/James_Anderson_(Freemason)" title="James Anderson (Freemason)">James Anderson's</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutions_of_the_Free-Masons" title="Constitutions of the Free-Masons">Constitutions of the Free-Masons</a></i>. Franklin remained a Freemason for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common-law_marriage_to_Deborah_Read">Common-law marriage to Deborah Read</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deborah_ReadFranklin.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="729" data-file-width="600" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Deborah_ReadFranklin.jpg/160px-Deborah_ReadFranklin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Deborah_ReadFranklin.jpg/240px-Deborah_ReadFranklin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Deborah_ReadFranklin.jpg/320px-Deborah_ReadFranklin.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Deborah_Read" title="Deborah Read">Deborah Read Franklin</a><br/>(c. 1759). Common-law wife of Benjamin Franklin</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sarah_Franklin_Bache1793.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1923" data-file-width="1586" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sarah_Franklin_Bache1793.jpg/160px-Sarah_Franklin_Bache1793.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sarah_Franklin_Bache1793.jpg/240px-Sarah_Franklin_Bache1793.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sarah_Franklin_Bache1793.jpg/320px-Sarah_Franklin_Bache1793.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Sarah_Franklin_Bache" title="Sarah Franklin Bache">Sarah Franklin Bache</a> (1743–1808). Daughter of Benjamin Franklin and Deborah Read</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>At age 17 in 1723, Franklin proposed to 15-year-old <a href="/wiki/Deborah_Read" title="Deborah Read">Deborah Read</a> while a boarder in the Read home. At that time, Read's mother was wary of allowing her young daughter to marry Franklin, who was on his way to London at Governor <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Keith,_4th_Baronet" title="Sir William Keith, 4th Baronet">Sir William Keith's</a> request, and also because of his financial instability. Her own husband had recently died, and she declined Franklin's request to marry her daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Franklin was in London, his trip was extended, and there were problems with Sir William's promises of support. Perhaps because of the circumstances of this delay, Deborah married a man named John Rodgers. This proved to be a regrettable decision. Rodgers shortly avoided his debts and prosecution by fleeing to <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> with her <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, leaving her behind. Rodgers's fate was unknown, and because of <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamy</a> laws, Deborah was not free to remarry.
</p><p>Franklin established a <a href="/wiki/Common-law_marriage" title="Common-law marriage">common-law marriage</a> with Deborah Read on September 1, 1730. They took in Franklin's recently acknowledged young illegitimate son <a href="/wiki/William_Franklin" title="William Franklin">William</a> and raised him in their household. They had two children together. Their son, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Folger_Franklin" title="Francis Folger Franklin">Francis Folger Franklin</a>, was born in October 1732 and died of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> in 1736. Their daughter, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Franklin_Bache" title="Sarah Franklin Bache">Sarah "Sally" Franklin</a>, was born in 1743 and grew up to marry <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bache" title="Richard Bache">Richard Bache</a>, have seven children, and look after her father in his old age.
</p><p>Deborah's fear of the sea meant that she never accompanied Franklin on any of his extended trips to Europe, and another possible reason why they spent so much time apart is that he may have blamed her for preventing their son Francis from being vaccinated against the disease that subsequently killed him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Deborah wrote to him in November 1769 saying she was ill due to "dissatisfied distress" from his prolonged absence, but he did not return until his business was done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Deborah Read Franklin died of a stroke in 1774, while Franklin was on an extended mission to England; he returned in 1775.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="William_Franklin">William Franklin</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/William_Franklin" title="William Franklin">William Franklin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WilliamFranklin.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="299" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/WilliamFranklin.jpeg/170px-WilliamFranklin.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/WilliamFranklin.jpeg/255px-WilliamFranklin.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/WilliamFranklin.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WilliamFranklin.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Franklin" title="William Franklin">William Franklin</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1730, 24-year-old Franklin publicly acknowledged the existence of his son William, who was deemed "illegitimate," as he was born out of wedlock, and raised him in his household. His mother's identity is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> He was educated in Philadelphia. Beginning at about age 30, William studied law in London in the early 1760s. He fathered an illegitimate son, <a href="/wiki/William_Temple_Franklin" title="William Temple Franklin">William Temple Franklin</a>, born February 22, 1762. The boy's mother was never identified, and he was placed in foster care. Later in 1762, William married Elizabeth Downes, daughter of a <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">planter</a> from <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>. After William passed the bar, his father helped him gain an appointment in 1763 as the last <a href="/wiki/List_of_colonial_governors_of_New_Jersey" title="List of colonial governors of New Jersey">Royal Governor</a> of New Jersey.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">Loyalist</a>, William and his father eventually broke relations over their differences about the American Revolutionary War. The elder Franklin could never accept William's position. Deposed in 1776 by the revolutionary government of New Jersey, William was arrested at his home in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perth_Amboy" title="Perth Amboy">Perth Amboy</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_House" title="Proprietary House">Proprietary House</a> and imprisoned for a time. The younger Franklin went to New York in 1782, which was still occupied by British troops. He became leader of the Board of Associated Loyalists—a quasi-military organization, headquartered in New York City. They initiated guerrilla forays into <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> counties north of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> When British troops evacuated from New York, William Franklin left with them and sailed to England. He settled in London, never to return to North America. In the preliminary peace talks in 1782 with Britain, "... Benjamin Franklin insisted that loyalists who had borne arms against the United States would be excluded from this plea (that they be given a general pardon). He was undoubtedly thinking of William Franklin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Success_as_an_author">Success as an author</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1,_January,_1741.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1692" data-file-width="962" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1%2C_January%2C_1741.jpg/170px-The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1%2C_January%2C_1741.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1%2C_January%2C_1741.jpg/255px-The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1%2C_January%2C_1741.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1%2C_January%2C_1741.jpg/340px-The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1%2C_January%2C_1741.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_General_Magazine_and_Historical_Chronicle_Vol_1,_January,_1741.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin's <i>The General Magazine and Historical Chronicle</i> (Jan. 1741)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1733, Franklin began to publish the noted <i><a href="/wiki/Poor_Richard%27s_Almanack" title="Poor Richard's Almanack">Poor Richard's Almanack</a></i> (with content both original and borrowed) under the pseudonym Richard Saunders, on which much of his popular reputation is based. Franklin frequently wrote under pseudonyms. Although it was no secret that Franklin was the author, his Richard Saunders character repeatedly denied it. "Poor Richard's Proverbs", adages from this almanac, such as "A penny saved is twopence dear" (often misquoted as "A penny saved is a penny earned") and "Fish and visitors stink in three days", remain common quotations in the modern world. Wisdom in folk society meant the ability to provide an apt adage for any occasion, and Franklin's readers became well prepared. He sold about ten thousand copies per year—it became an institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Doren1938109_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Doren1938109-41">[41]</a></sup> In 1741 Franklin began publishing <i>The General Magazine and Historical Chronicle for all the British Plantations in America</i>, the first such monthly magazine of this type published in America.
</p><p>In 1758, the year he ceased writing for the Almanack, he printed <i>Father Abraham's Sermon</i>, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Way_to_Wealth" title="The Way to Wealth">The Way to Wealth</a></i>. Franklin's <a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Benjamin_Franklin" title="The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin">autobiography</a>, begun in 1771 but published after his death, has become one of the classics of the genre.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time" title="Daylight saving time">Daylight saving time</a> (DST) is often erroneously attributed to a 1784 satire that Franklin published <a href="/wiki/Anonymity" title="Anonymity">anonymously</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Modern DST was first proposed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Vernon_Hudson" title="George Vernon Hudson">George Vernon Hudson</a> in 1895.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inventions_and_scientific_inquiries">Inventions and scientific inquiries</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Social_contributions_and_studies_by_Benjamin_Franklin" title="Social contributions and studies by Benjamin Franklin">Social contributions and studies by Benjamin Franklin</a></div>
<p>Franklin was a prodigious inventor. Among his many creations were the <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning rod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glass_harmonica" title="Glass harmonica">glass harmonica</a> (a glass instrument, not to be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Harmonica" title="Harmonica">metal harmonica</a>), <a href="/wiki/Franklin_stove" title="Franklin stove">Franklin stove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bifocals" title="Bifocals">bifocal glasses</a> and the flexible <a href="/wiki/Urinary_catheterization" title="Urinary catheterization">urinary catheter</a>. Franklin never patented his inventions; in his <a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Benjamin_Franklin" title="The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin">autobiography</a> he wrote, "... as we enjoy great advantages from the inventions of others, we should be glad of an opportunity to serve others by any invention of ours; and this we should do freely and generously."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electricity">Electricity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_West,_English_(born_America)_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5413" data-file-width="4039" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Benjamin_West%2C_English_%28born_America%29_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Benjamin_West%2C_English_%28born_America%29_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Benjamin_West%2C_English_%28born_America%29_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Benjamin_West%2C_English_%28born_America%29_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Benjamin_West%2C_English_%28born_America%29_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Benjamin_West%2C_English_%28born_America%29_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_West,_English_(born_America)_-_Benjamin_Franklin_Drawing_Electricity_from_the_Sky_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Benjamin Franklin Drawing Electricity from the Sky</i> c. 1816 at the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art" title="Philadelphia Museum of Art">Philadelphia Museum of Art</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_West" title="Benjamin West">Benjamin West</a></div></div></div>
<p>Franklin started exploring the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> in 1746 when he saw some of <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Spencer" title="Archibald Spencer">Archibald Spencer</a>'s lectures using static electricity for illustrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyColonial_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyColonial-45">[45]</a></sup> Franklin proposed that "vitreous" and "resinous" electricity were not different types of "<a href="/wiki/Aether_theories" title="Aether theories">electrical fluid</a>" (as electricity was called then), but the same "fluid" under different pressures. (The same proposal was made independently that same year by <a href="/wiki/William_Watson_(scientist)" title="William Watson (scientist)">William Watson</a>.) Franklin was the first to label them as <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">positive and negative</a> respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and he was the first to discover the principle of <a href="/wiki/Charge_conservation" title="Charge conservation">conservation of charge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In 1748 he constructed a multiple plate capacitor, that he called an "electrical battery" (not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Volta's</a> <a href="/wiki/Voltaic_pile" title="Voltaic pile">pile</a>) by placing eleven panes of glass sandwiched between lead plates, suspended with silk cords and connected by wires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In recognition of his work with electricity, Franklin received the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Copley_Medal" title="Copley Medal">Copley Medal</a> in 1753, and in 1756 he became one of the few 18th-century Americans elected as a Fellow of the Society. He received honorary degrees from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvard" title="Harvard">Harvard</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yale" title="Yale">Yale</a> universities (his first).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVa_Doren1938168_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVa_Doren1938168-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">cgs</a> unit of electric charge has been named after him: one <i>franklin</i> (Fr) is equal to one <a href="/wiki/Statcoulomb" title="Statcoulomb">statcoulomb</a>.
</p><p>Franklin advised Harvard University in its acquisition of new electrical laboratory apparatus after the complete loss of its original collection, in a fire which destroyed the original <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Hall" title="Harvard Hall">Harvard Hall</a> in 1764. The collection he assembled would later become part of the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Collection_of_Historical_Scientific_Instruments" title="Harvard Collection of Historical Scientific Instruments">Harvard Collection of Historical Scientific Instruments</a>, now on public display in its <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Science_Center" title="Harvard Science Center">Science Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomase_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomase-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin briefly investigated <a href="/wiki/Electrotherapy" title="Electrotherapy">electrotherapy</a>, including the use of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_bath_(electrotherapy)" title="Electric bath (electrotherapy)">electric bath</a>.  This work led to the field becoming widely known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kite_experiment_and_lightning_rod">Kite experiment and lightning rod</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kite_experiment" title="Kite experiment">Kite experiment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9600" data-file-width="8160" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg/220px-BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg/330px-BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg/440px-BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BEP-JONES-Franklin_and_Electricity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Franklin and Electricity</i> vignette <a href="/wiki/Art_and_engraving_on_United_States_banknotes" title="Art and engraving on United States banknotes">engraved</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> (c. 1860)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1750,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> he published a proposal for an experiment to prove that <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> is electricity by <a href="/wiki/Kite_experiment" title="Kite experiment">flying a kite in a storm</a> that appeared capable of becoming a lightning storm. On May 10, 1752, <a href="/wiki/Thomas-Fran%C3%A7ois_Dalibard" title="Thomas-François Dalibard">Thomas-François Dalibard</a> of France conducted Franklin's experiment using a 40-foot-tall (12 m) iron rod instead of a kite, and he extracted electrical sparks from a cloud. On June 15 Franklin may possibly have conducted his well-known kite experiment <a href="/wiki/St._Stephen%27s_Episcopal_Church_(Philadelphia)" title="St. Stephen's Episcopal Church (Philadelphia)">in Philadelphia</a>, successfully extracting sparks from a cloud. Franklin described the experiment in the <i><a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Gazette" title="Pennsylvania Gazette">Pennsylvania Gazette</a></i> on October 19, 1752,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> without mentioning that he himself had performed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> This account was read to the Royal Society on December 21 and printed as such in the <i>Philosophical Transactions</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archives_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-archives-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> published an account with additional details in his 1767 <i>History and Present Status of Electricity</i>. Franklin was careful to stand on an insulator, keeping dry under a roof to avoid the danger of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_shock" title="Electric shock">electric shock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Doren1938159_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Doren1938159-57">[57]</a></sup> Others, such as Prof. <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Richmann" title="Georg Wilhelm Richmann">Georg Wilhelm Richmann</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, were indeed electrocuted in performing lightning experiments during the months immediately following Franklin's experiment.
</p><p>In his writings, Franklin indicates that he was aware of the dangers and offered alternative ways to demonstrate that lightning was electrical, as shown by his use of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">electrical ground</a>. Franklin did not perform this experiment in the way that is often pictured in popular literature, flying the kite and waiting to be struck by lightning, as it would have been dangerous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Instead he used the kite to collect some electric charge from a storm cloud, showing that lightning was electrical.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> On October 19 in a letter to England with directions for repeating the experiment, Franklin wrote:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When rain has wet the kite twine so that it can conduct the electric fire freely, you will find it streams out plentifully from the key at the approach of your knuckle, and with this key a phial, or <a href="/wiki/Leyden_jar" title="Leyden jar">Leyden jar</a>, may be charged: and from electric fire thus obtained spirits may be kindled, and all other electric experiments [may be] performed which are usually done by the help of a rubber glass globe or tube; and therefore the sameness of the electrical matter with that of lightening completely demonstrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Franklin's electrical experiments led to his invention of the <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning rod</a>. He said that conductors with a sharp<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> rather than a smooth point could discharge silently, and at a far greater distance. He surmised that this could help protect buildings from lightning by attaching "upright Rods of Iron, made sharp as a Needle and gilt to prevent Rusting, and from the Foot of those Rods a Wire down the outside of the Building into the Ground; ... Would not these pointed Rods probably draw the Electrical Fire silently out of a Cloud before it came nigh enough to strike, and thereby secure us from that most sudden and terrible Mischief!" Following a series of experiments on Franklin's own house, lightning rods were installed on the Academy of Philadelphia (later the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>) and the Pennsylvania State House (later Independence Hall) in 1752.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_studies">Population studies</span></h3>
<p>Franklin had a major influence on the emerging science of <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demography</a>, or population studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> is noted for his rule of population growth and credited Franklin for discovering it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Kammen (1990) and Drake (2011) say Franklin's "<a href="/wiki/Observations_Concerning_the_Increase_of_Mankind,_Peopling_of_Countries,_etc." title="Observations Concerning the Increase of Mankind, Peopling of Countries, etc.">Observations on the Increase of Mankind</a>" (1755) stands alongside <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles" title="Ezra Stiles">Ezra Stiles</a>' "Discourse on Christian Union" (1760) as the leading works of eighteenth-century Anglo-American demography; Drake credits Franklin's "wide readership and prophetic insight."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1730s and 1740s, Franklin began taking notes on population growth, finding that the American population had the fastest growth rate on earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Emphasizing that population growth depended on food supplies—a line of thought later developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a>—Franklin emphasized the abundance of food and available farmland in America. He calculated that America's population was doubling every twenty years and would surpass that of England in a century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In 1751, he drafted "Observations concerning the Increase of Mankind, Peopling of Countries, &amp;c." Four years later, it was anonymously printed in Boston, and it was quickly reproduced in Britain, where it influenced the economist <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> and later the demographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a>. Franklin's predictions alarmed British leaders who did not want to be surpassed by the colonies, so they became more willing to impose restrictions on the colonial economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin was also a pioneer in the study of slave demography, as shown in his 1755 essay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_Ocean_currents">Atlantic Ocean currents</span></h3>
<p>As deputy postmaster, Franklin became interested in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">North Atlantic Ocean</a> circulation patterns. While in England in 1768, he heard a complaint from the Colonial Board of Customs: Why did it take British packet ships carrying mail several weeks longer to reach New York than it took an average merchant ship to reach <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>? The merchantmen had a longer and more complex voyage because they left from London, while the packets left from <a href="/wiki/Falmouth,_Cornwall" title="Falmouth, Cornwall">Falmouth</a> in Cornwall.
</p><p>Franklin put the question to his cousin Timothy Folger, a <a href="/wiki/Nantucket" title="Nantucket">Nantucket</a> whaler captain, who told him that merchant ships routinely avoided a strong eastbound mid-ocean current. The mail packet captains sailed dead into it, thus fighting an adverse current of 3 miles per hour (5 km/h). Franklin worked with Folger and other experienced ship captains, learning enough to chart the current and name it the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>, by which it is still known today.
</p><p>Franklin published his Gulf Stream chart in 1770 in England, where it was completely ignored. Subsequent versions were printed in France in 1778 and the U.S. in 1786. The British edition of the chart, which was the original, was so thoroughly ignored that everyone assumed it was lost forever until Phil Richardson, a <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Oceanographic_Institution" title="Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution">Woods Hole oceanographer</a> and Gulf Stream expert, discovered it in the <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque nationale de France">Bibliothèque Nationale</a> in Paris in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> This find received front-page coverage in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>It took many years for British sea captains to adopt Franklin's advice on navigating the current; once they did, they were able to trim two weeks from their sailing time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In 1853, the oceanographer and cartographer <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fontaine_Maury" title="Matthew Fontaine Maury">Matthew Fontaine Maury</a> noted that while Franklin charted and codified the Gulf Stream, he did not discover it:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Though it was Dr. Franklin and Captain Tim Folger, who first turned the Gulf Stream to nautical account, the discovery that there was a Gulf Stream cannot be said to belong to either of them, for its existence was known to <a href="/wiki/Peter_Martyr_d%27Anghiera" title="Peter Martyr d'Anghiera">Peter Martyr d'Anghiera</a>, and to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Humphrey_Gilbert" title="Sir Humphrey Gilbert">Sir Humphrey Gilbert</a>, in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_theory_of_light">Wave theory of light</span></h3>
<p>Franklin was, along with his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a>, the only major scientist who supported <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_theory_of_light" title="Wave theory of light">wave theory of light</a>, which was basically ignored by the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_community" title="Scientific community">scientific community</a>. In the 18th century <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton's</a> <a href="/wiki/Corpuscular_theory_of_light" title="Corpuscular theory of light">corpuscular theory</a> was held to be true; only after <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Young's</a> well-known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slit_experiment" title="Slit experiment">slit experiment</a> in 1803 were most scientists persuaded to believe Huygens's theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorology">Meteorology</span></h3>
<p>On October 21, 1743, according to popular myth, a storm moving from the southwest denied Franklin the opportunity of witnessing a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse</a>. Franklin was said to have noted that the <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">prevailing winds</a> were actually from the northeast, contrary to what he had expected. In correspondence with his brother, Franklin learned that the same storm had not reached Boston until after the eclipse, despite the fact that Boston is to the northeast of Philadelphia. He deduced that storms do not always travel in the direction of the prevailing wind, a concept that greatly influenced <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the Icelandic volcanic eruption of <a href="/wiki/Laki" title="Laki">Laki</a> in 1783, and the subsequent harsh European winter of 1784, Franklin made observations connecting the causal nature of these two separate events. He wrote about them in a lecture series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traction_kiting">Traction kiting</span></h3>
<p>Though Benjamin Franklin has been most noted kite-wise with his lightning experiments, he has also been noted by many for his using kites to pull humans and ships across waterways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/George_Pocock_(inventor)" title="George Pocock (inventor)">George Pocock</a> in the book <i>A TREATISE on The Aeropleustic Art, or Navigation in the Air, by means of Kites, or Buoyant Sails</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> noted being inspired by Benjamin Franklin's traction of his body by kite power across a waterway. In his later years he suggested using the technique for pulling ships.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_of_cooling">Concept of cooling</span></h3>
<p>Franklin noted a principle of <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">refrigeration</a> by observing that on a very hot day, he stayed cooler in a wet shirt in a breeze than he did in a dry one. To understand this phenomenon more clearly Franklin conducted experiments. In 1758 on a warm day in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>, England, Franklin and fellow scientist <a href="/wiki/John_Hadley_(chemist)" title="John Hadley (chemist)">John Hadley</a> experimented by continually wetting the ball of a mercury <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometer</a> with <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a> and using <a href="/wiki/Bellows" title="Bellows">bellows</a> to evaporate the ether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> With each subsequent <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, the thermometer read a lower temperature, eventually reaching 7 °F (−14 °C). Another thermometer showed that the room temperature was constant at 65 °F (18 °C). In his letter <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooling_by_Evaporation" title="Cooling by Evaporation">Cooling by Evaporation</a></i>, Franklin noted that, "One may see the possibility of freezing a man to death on a warm summer's day."
</p>
<h3><span id="Temperature.27s_effect_on_electrical_conductivity"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature's_effect_on_electrical_conductivity">Temperature's effect on electrical conductivity</span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>, Franklin's experiments on the non-conduction of ice are worth mentioning, although the law of the general effect of liquefaction on electrolytes is not attributed to Franklin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> However, as reported in 1836 by Prof. A. D. Bache of the University of Pennsylvania, the law of the effect of heat on the conduction of bodies otherwise non-conductors, for example, glass, could be attributed to Franklin. Franklin writes, "... A certain quantity of heat will make some bodies good conductors, that will not otherwise conduct ..." and again, "... And water, though naturally a good conductor, will not conduct well when frozen into ice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanography_findings">Oceanography findings</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1732" data-file-width="1112" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg/220px-BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg/330px-BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg/440px-BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BenFranklin_Waterspout_1806.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration from Franklin's paper on "<a href="/wiki/Waterspout" title="Waterspout">Water-spouts</a> and Whirlwinds"</div></div></div>
<p>An aging Franklin accumulated all his oceanographic findings in <i>Maritime Observations</i>, published by the Philosophical Society's <i>transactions</i> in 1786.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> It contained ideas for <a href="/wiki/Sea_anchor" title="Sea anchor">sea anchors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catamaran" title="Catamaran">catamaran</a> hulls, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watertight_compartment" title="Watertight compartment">watertight compartments</a>, shipboard lightning rods and a soup bowl designed to stay stable in stormy weather.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decision-making">Decision-making</span></h3>
<p>In a 1772 letter to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a>, Franklin lays out the earliest known description of the Pro &amp; Con list,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decisions_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-decisions-85">[85]</a></sup> a common <a href="/wiki/Decision-making" title="Decision-making">decision-making</a> technique, now sometimes called a <a href="/wiki/Decisional_balance_sheet" title="Decisional balance sheet">decisional balance sheet</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>... my Way is, to divide half a Sheet of Paper by a Line into two Columns, writing over the one <i>Pro</i>, and over the other <i>Con</i>. Then during three or four Days Consideration I put down under the different Heads short Hints of the different Motives that at different Times occur to me for or against the Measure. When I have thus got them all together in one View, I endeavour to estimate their respective Weights; and where I find two, one on each side, that seem equal, I strike them both out: If I find a Reason <i>pro</i> equal to some two Reasons <i>con</i>, I strike out the three. If I judge some two Reasons <i>con</i> equal to some three Reasons <i>pro</i>, I strike out the five; and thus proceeding I find at length where the Ballance lies; and if after a Day or two of farther Consideration nothing new that is of Importance occurs on either side, I come to a Determination accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decisions_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-decisions-85">[85]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><span id="Science_humor"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_on_water">Oil on water</span></h3>
<p>While traveling on a ship, Franklin had observed that the wake of a ship <a href="/wiki/Storm_oil" title="Storm oil">was diminished</a> when the cooks scuttled their greasy water. He studied the effects on a large pond in <a href="/wiki/Clapham_Common" title="Clapham Common">Clapham Common</a>, London. "I fetched out a cruet of oil and dropt a little of it on the water ... though not more than a teaspoon full, produced an instant calm over a space of several yards square." He later used the trick to "calm the waters" by carrying "a little oil in the hollow joint of my cane".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_endeavors">Musical endeavors</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glassharmonica.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="217" data-file-width="253" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Glassharmonica.png/220px-Glassharmonica.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/Glassharmonica.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glassharmonica.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Glass_harmonica" title="Glass harmonica">Glass harmonica</a></div></div></div>
<p>Franklin is known to have played the violin, the <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, and the guitar. He also composed music, notably a <a href="/wiki/String_quartet" title="String quartet">string quartet</a> in <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">early classical style</a>. While he was in London, he developed a much-improved version of the <a href="/wiki/Glass_harmonica" title="Glass harmonica">glass harmonica</a>, in which the glasses rotate on a shaft, with the player's fingers held steady, instead of the other way around. He worked with the London glassblower Charles James to create it, and instruments based on his mechanical version soon found their way to other parts of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joesph_Haydn" title="Joesph Haydn">Joesph Haydn</a> (a fan of Franklin's enlightened ideas) had a glass harmonica in his instrument collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beethoven" title="Beethoven">Beethoven</a> wrote a sonata for the glass harmonica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chess">Chess</span></h2>
<p>Franklin was an avid <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a> player.
