He was playing chess by around 1733, making him the first chess player known by name in the American colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCraryChessandFranklin_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCraryChessandFranklin-91">[91]</a></sup> His essay on "<a href="/wiki/The_Morals_of_Chess" title="The Morals of Chess">The Morals of Chess</a>" in <i>Columbian</i> magazine in December 1786 is the second known writing on chess in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCraryChessandFranklin_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCraryChessandFranklin-91">[91]</a></sup> This essay in praise of chess and prescribing a code of behavior for the game has been widely reprinted and translated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> He and a friend also used chess as a means of learning the <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian language</a>, which both were studying; the winner of each game between them had the right to assign a task, such as parts of the Italian grammar to be learned by heart, to be performed by the loser before their next meeting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin was able to play chess more frequently against stronger opposition during his many years as a civil servant and diplomat in England, where the game was far better established than in America. He was able to improve his playing standard by facing more experienced players during this period. He regularly attended <a href="/wiki/Old_Slaughter%27s_Coffee_House" title="Old Slaughter's Coffee House">Old Slaughter's Coffee House</a> in London for chess and socializing, making many important personal contacts. While in Paris, both as a visitor and later as ambassador, he visited the famous <a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_de_la_R%C3%A9gence" title="Café de la Régence">Café de la Régence</a>, which France's strongest players made their regular meeting place. No records of his games have survived, so it is not possible to ascertain his playing strength in modern terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/World_Chess_Hall_of_Fame" title="World Chess Hall of Fame">U.S. Chess Hall of Fame</a> in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCraryChessandFranklin_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-McCraryChessandFranklin-91">[91]</a></sup> The Franklin Mercantile Chess Club in Philadelphia, the second oldest chess club in the U.S., is named in his honor.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_life">Public life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_steps_in_Pennsylvania">Early steps in Pennsylvania</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2696" data-file-width="3740" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg/220px-Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg/330px-Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg/440px-Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_-_Join_or_Die.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Join,_or_Die" title="Join, or Die">Join, or Die</a>: This political cartoon by Franklin urged the colonies to join together during the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> (<a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>In 1736, Franklin created the <a href="/wiki/Union_Fire_Company" title="Union Fire Company">Union Fire Company</a>, one of the first <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_fire_department" title="Volunteer fire department">volunteer firefighting companies</a> in America. In the same year, he printed a new currency for <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> based on innovative anti-<a href="/wiki/Counterfeit" title="Counterfeit">counterfeiting</a> techniques he had devised. Throughout his career, Franklin was an advocate for <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">paper money</a>, publishing <i>A Modest Enquiry into the Nature and Necessity of a Paper Currency</i> in 1729, and his printer printed money. He was influential in the more restrained and thus successful monetary experiments in the Middle Colonies, which stopped <a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">deflation</a> without causing excessive inflation. In 1766 he made a case for paper money to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">British House of Commons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>As he matured, Franklin began to concern himself more with public affairs. In 1743, he first devised a scheme for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Academy_and_College_of_Philadelphia" title="The Academy and College of Philadelphia">The Academy, Charity School, and College of Philadelphia</a>. However, the person he had in mind to run the academy, Rev. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Peters_(priest)" title="Richard Peters (priest)">Richard Peters</a>, refused and Franklin put his ideas away until 1749, when he printed his own pamphlet, <i>Proposals Relating to the Education of Youth in Pensilvania.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MontgomeryHistory_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-MontgomeryHistory-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>30</span></sup> He was appointed president of the Academy on November 13, 1749; the Academy and the Charity School opened on August 13, 1751.
</p><p>In 1743, Franklin founded the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> to help scientific men discuss their discoveries and theories. He began the electrical research that, along with other scientific inquiries, would occupy him for the rest of his life, in between bouts of politics and moneymaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1747, Franklin (already a very wealthy man) retired from printing and went into other businesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> He created a partnership with his foreman, <a href="/wiki/David_Hall_(publisher)" title="David Hall (publisher)">David Hall</a>, which provided Franklin with half of the shop's profits for 18 years. This lucrative business arrangement provided leisure time for study, and in a few years he had made discoveries that gave him a reputation with educated persons throughout Europe and especially in France.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="600" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg/260px-PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg/390px-PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg/520px-PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PennsylvaniaHospitalWilliamStrickland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Hospital" title="Pennsylvania Hospital">Pennsylvania Hospital</a> by <a href="/wiki/William_Strickland_(architect)" title="William Strickland (architect)">William Strickland</a>, 1755</div></div></div>
<p>Franklin became involved in Philadelphia politics and rapidly progressed. In October 1748, he was selected as a councilman, in June 1749 he became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_Peace" title="Justice of the Peace">Justice of the Peace</a> for Philadelphia, and in 1751 he was elected to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Provincial_Assembly" title="Pennsylvania Provincial Assembly">Pennsylvania Assembly</a>. On August 10, 1753, Franklin was appointed deputy postmaster-general of <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin#Postmaster" title="Benjamin Franklin">see below</a>). His most notable service in domestic politics was his reform of the postal system, with mail sent out every week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1751, Franklin and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bond_(physician)" title="Thomas Bond (physician)">Dr. Thomas Bond</a> obtained a charter from the Pennsylvania legislature to establish a hospital. <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Hospital" title="Pennsylvania Hospital">Pennsylvania Hospital</a> was the first hospital in what was to become the United States of America.
</p><p>In 1752, Franklin organized the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Contributionship" title="Philadelphia Contributionship">Philadelphia Contributionship</a>, the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeowner%27s_insurance" title="Homeowner's insurance">homeowner's insurance</a> company in what would become the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1757_UPenn_Seal.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1038" data-file-width="1025" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1757_UPenn_Seal.png/170px-1757_UPenn_Seal.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1757_UPenn_Seal.png/255px-1757_UPenn_Seal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1757_UPenn_Seal.png/340px-1757_UPenn_Seal.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1757_UPenn_Seal.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seal of the College of Philadelphia</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1750 and 1753, the "educational triumvirate"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> of Dr. Benjamin Franklin, the American <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson_(American_educator)" title="Samuel Johnson (American educator)">Dr. Samuel Johnson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stratford,_Connecticut" title="Stratford, Connecticut">Stratford, Connecticut</a>, and the immigrant Scottish schoolteacher <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(Episcopal_priest)" title="William Smith (Episcopal priest)">Dr. William Smith</a> built on Franklin's initial scheme and created what <a href="/wiki/James_Madison_(bishop)" title="James Madison (bishop)">Bishop James Madison</a>, president of the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a>, called a "new-model"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> plan or style of American college. Franklin solicited, printed in 1752, and promoted an American textbook of <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">moral philosophy</a> from the American <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson_(American_educator)" title="Samuel Johnson (American educator)">Dr. Samuel Johnson</a> titled <i>Elementa Philosophica</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> to be taught in the new colleges to replace courses in denominational divinity.
</p><p>In June 1753, Johnson, Franklin, and Smith met in Stratford.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> They decided the new-model college would focus on the professions, with classes taught in English instead of Latin, have subject matter experts as professors instead of one tutor leading a class for four years, and there would be no religious test for admission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Johnson went on to found King's College (now <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>) in New York City in 1754, while Franklin hired Smith as Provost of the College of Philadelphia, which opened in 1755. At its first commencement, on May 17, 1757, seven men graduated; six with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> and one as <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts" title="Master of Arts">Master of Arts</a>. It was later merged with the University of the State of Pennsylvania to become the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>. The College was to become influential in guiding <a href="/wiki/Template:Founding_Documents_of_America" title="Template:Founding Documents of America">the founding documents of the United States</a>: in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a>, for example, over one third of the college-affiliated men who contributed the <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a></i> between September 4, 1774, and July 4, 1776, were affiliated with the College.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1753, both <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> awarded him honorary degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1043" data-file-width="1466" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg/220px-Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg/330px-Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg/440px-Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sketch_of_Tun_Tavern_in_the_Revolutionary_War.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sketch of the original <a href="/wiki/Tun_Tavern" title="Tun Tavern">Tun Tavern</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1754, he headed the Pennsylvania delegation to the <a href="/wiki/Albany_Congress" title="Albany Congress">Albany Congress</a>. This meeting of several colonies had been requested by the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a> in England to improve relations with the Indians and defense against the French. Franklin proposed a broad <a href="/wiki/Albany_Plan" title="Albany Plan">Plan of Union</a> for the colonies. While the plan was not adopted, elements of it found their way into the <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a>.
</p><p>In 1756, Franklin received an honorary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Master_of_arts" title="Master of arts">master of arts</a> degree from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" title="College of William and Mary">College of William and Mary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Later in 1756, Franklin organized the Pennsylvania Militia (see "Associated Regiment of Philadelphia" under heading of Pennsylvania's 103rd Artillery and <a href="/wiki/111th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="111th Infantry Regiment (United States)">111th Infantry Regiment</a> at <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>). He used <a href="/wiki/Tun_Tavern" title="Tun Tavern">Tun Tavern</a> as a gathering place to recruit a regiment of soldiers to go into battle against the <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> uprisings that beset the American colonies. Reportedly Franklin was elected "Colonel" of the Associated Regiment but declined the honor.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decades_in_London">Decades in London</span></h3>
<p>From the mid 1750s to the mid 1770s, Franklin spent much of his time in London. Officially he was there on a political mission, but he used his time to further his scientific explorations as well, meeting many notable people.
</p><p>In 1757, he was sent to England by the Pennsylvania Assembly as a colonial agent to protest against the political influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penn_family" title="Penn family">Penn family</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_colony" title="Proprietary colony">proprietors of the colony</a>. He remained there for five years, striving to end the proprietors' prerogative to overturn legislation from the elected Assembly, and their exemption from paying taxes on their land. His lack of influential allies in <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a> led to the failure of this mission.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US-Colonial_(PA-115)-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2453" data-file-width="4172" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/US-Colonial_%28PA-115%29-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg/220px-US-Colonial_%28PA-115%29-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/US-Colonial_%28PA-115%29-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg/330px-US-Colonial_%28PA-115%29-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/US-Colonial_%28PA-115%29-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg/440px-US-Colonial_%28PA-115%29-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US-Colonial_(PA-115)-Pennsylvania-18_Jun_1764.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pennsylvania colonial currency printed by Franklin in 1764</div></div></div>
<p>At this time, many members of the Pennsylvania Assembly were feuding with <a href="/wiki/List_of_colonial_governors_of_Pennsylvania#Proprietors" title="List of colonial governors of Pennsylvania">William Penn's heirs</a>, who controlled the colony as proprietors. After his return to the colony, Franklin led the "anti-proprietary party" in the struggle against the Penn family, and was elected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_Pennsylvania_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the Pennsylvania House of Representatives">Speaker of the Pennsylvania House</a> in May 1764. His call for a change from proprietary to royal government was a rare political miscalculation, however: Pennsylvanians worried that such a move would endanger their political and religious freedoms. Because of these fears, and because of political attacks on his character, Franklin lost his seat in the October 1764 Assembly elections. The anti-proprietary party dispatched Franklin to England again to continue the struggle against the Penn family proprietorship. During this trip, events drastically changed the nature of his mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANB_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANB-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>In London, Franklin opposed the <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act_1765" title="Stamp Act 1765">1765 Stamp Act</a>. Unable to prevent its passage, he made another political miscalculation and recommended a friend to the post of stamp distributor for Pennsylvania. Pennsylvanians were outraged, believing that he had supported the measure all along, and threatened to destroy his home in Philadelphia. Franklin soon learned of the extent of colonial resistance to the Stamp Act, and he testified during the House of Commons proceedings that led to its repeal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2386" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg/340px-Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_1767.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin in London, 1767, wearing a blue suit with elaborate gold braid and buttons, a far cry from the simple dress he affected at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Court_(royal)" title="Court (royal)">French court</a> in later years. Painting by <a href="/wiki/David_Martin_(artist)" title="David Martin (artist)">David Martin</a>, displayed in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>With this, Franklin suddenly emerged as the leading spokesman for American interests in England. He wrote popular essays on behalf of the colonies. <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> also appointed him as their agent to the Crown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANB_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-ANB-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin lodged in a house in Craven Street, just off <a href="/wiki/Strand,_London" title="Strand, London">The Strand</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">central London</a>. During his stays there, he developed a close friendship with his landlady, Margaret Stevenson, and her circle of friends and relations, in particular her daughter Mary, who was more often known as Polly. Their house, which he used on various lengthy missions from 1757 to 1775, is the only one of his residences to survive. It opened to the public as the <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_House" title="Benjamin Franklin House">Benjamin Franklin House</a> museum in 2006.
</p><p>Whilst in London, Franklin became involved in radical politics. He belonged to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gentleman%27s_club" title="Gentleman's club">gentleman's club</a> (which he called "the honest Whigs"), which held stated meetings, and included members such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Price" title="Richard Price">Richard Price</a>, the minister of <a href="/wiki/Newington_Green_Unitarian_Church" title="Newington Green Unitarian Church">Newington Green Unitarian Church</a> who ignited the <a href="/wiki/Revolution_Controversy" title="Revolution Controversy">Revolution Controversy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Kippis" title="Andrew Kippis">Andrew Kippis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1756, Franklin had become a member of the Society for the Encouragement of Arts, Manufactures &amp; Commerce (now the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Society of Arts">Royal Society of Arts</a> or RSA), which had been founded in 1754 and whose early meetings took place in <a href="/wiki/English_coffeehouses_in_the_17th_and_18th_centuries" title="English coffeehouses in the 17th and 18th centuries">Covent Garden coffee shops</a>. After his return to the United States in 1775, Franklin became the Society's Corresponding Member, continuing a close connection. The RSA instituted a <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Medal_(Royal_Society_of_Arts)" title="Benjamin Franklin Medal (Royal Society of Arts)">Benjamin Franklin Medal</a> in 1956 to commemorate the 250th anniversary of his birth and the 200th anniversary of his membership of the RSA.
</p><p>The study of <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> (what we would call science) drew him into overlapping circles of acquaintance. Franklin was, for example, a corresponding member of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Society_of_Birmingham" title="Lunar Society of Birmingham">Lunar Society of Birmingham</a>, which included such other scientific and industrial luminaries as <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Boulton" title="Matthew Boulton">Matthew Boulton</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood" title="Josiah Wedgwood">Josiah Wedgwood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin" title="Erasmus Darwin">Erasmus Darwin</a>; on occasion he visited them.
</p><p>In 1759, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St_Andrews" title="University of St Andrews">University of St Andrews</a> awarded Franklin an <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">honorary doctorate</a> in recognition of his accomplishments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> He was also awarded an honorary doctorate by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> in 1762. Because of these honors, Franklin was often addressed as "Dr. Franklin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin also managed to secure an appointed post for his illegitimate son, <a href="/wiki/William_Franklin" title="William Franklin">William Franklin</a>, by then an attorney, as <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Jersey" title="Governor of New Jersey">Colonial Governor of New Jersey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>While living in London in 1768, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin%27s_phonetic_alphabet" title="Benjamin Franklin's phonetic alphabet">he developed a phonetic alphabet</a> in <i>A Scheme for a new Alphabet and a Reformed Mode of Spelling</i>. This reformed alphabet discarded six letters Franklin regarded as redundant (c, j, q, w, x, and y), and substituted six new letters for sounds he felt lacked letters of their own. This alphabet never caught on, and he eventually lost interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Travels_around_Britain_and_Ireland">Travels around Britain and Ireland</span></h3>
<p>Franklin used London as a base to travel. In 1771, he made short journeys through different parts of England, staying with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> at <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Percival" title="Thomas Percival">Thomas Percival</a> at <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin" title="Erasmus Darwin">Erasmus Darwin</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lichfield" title="Lichfield">Lichfield</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparks_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-sparks-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Scotland, he spent five days with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Home,_Lord_Kames" title="Henry Home, Lord Kames">Lord Kames</a> near <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a> and stayed for three weeks with <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> in Edinburgh. In 1759, he visited <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> with his son, and recalled his conversations there as "the <i>densest</i> happiness of my life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In February 1759, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St_Andrews" title="University of St Andrews">University of St Andrews</a> awarded him an honorary Doctor of Laws degree. From then he was known as "Doctor Franklin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In October of the same year he was granted <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_City" title="Freedom of the City">Freedom of the Borough</a> of <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews" title="St Andrews">St Andrews</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>He had never been to Ireland before, and met and stayed with <a href="/wiki/Wills_Hill,_1st_Marquess_of_Downshire" title="Wills Hill, 1st Marquess of Downshire">Lord Hillsborough</a>, who he believed was especially attentive. Franklin noted of him that "all the plausible behaviour I have described is meant only, by patting and stroking the horse, to make him more patient, while the reins are drawn tighter, and the spurs set deeper into his sides."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, Franklin was invited to sit with the members of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Irish Parliament</a> rather than in the gallery. He was the first American to receive this honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparks_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-sparks-119">[119]</a></sup> While touring Ireland, he was moved by the level of poverty he saw. Ireland's economy was affected by the same trade regulations and laws of Britain that governed America. Franklin feared that America could suffer the same effects should Britain's "colonial exploitation" continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visits_to_Europe">Visits to Europe</span></h3>
<p>Franklin spent two months in German lands in 1766, but his connections to the country stretched across a lifetime. He declared a debt of gratitude to German scientist <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke" title="Otto von Guericke">Otto von Guericke</a> for his early studies of electricity. Franklin also co-authored the first <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Amity_and_Commerce_(Prussia%E2%80%93United_States)" title="Treaty of Amity and Commerce (Prussia–United States)">treaty of friendship</a> between Prussia and America in 1785.
</p><p>In September 1767, Franklin visited Paris with his usual traveling partner, <a href="/wiki/John_Pringle_(physician)" title="John Pringle (physician)">Sir John Pringle</a>. News of his electrical discoveries was widespread in France. His reputation meant that he was introduced to many influential scientists and politicians, and also to <a href="/wiki/Louis_XV_of_France" title="Louis XV of France">King Louis XV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isaacson_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-isaacson-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defending_the_American_cause">Defending the American cause</span></h3>
<p>One line of argument in Parliament was that Americans should pay a share of the costs of the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>, and that therefore taxes should be levied on them. Franklin became the American spokesman in highly publicized testimony in Parliament in 1766. He stated that Americans already contributed heavily to the defense of the Empire. He said local governments had raised, outfitted and paid 25,000 soldiers to fight France—as many as Britain itself sent—and spent many millions from American treasuries doing so in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1773, Franklin published two of his most celebrated pro-American satirical essays: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Rules_By_Which_A_Great_Empire_May_Be_Reduced_To_A_Small_One" title="s:Rules By Which A Great Empire May Be Reduced To A Small One">"Rules by Which a Great Empire May Be Reduced to a Small One"</a>, and "An Edict by the King of Prussia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hutchinson_letters_leak">Hutchinson letters leak</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hutchinson_Letters_Affair" title="Hutchinson Letters Affair">Hutchinson Letters Affair</a></div>
<p>In 1772, Franklin obtained private letters of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hutchinson_(governor)" title="Thomas Hutchinson (governor)">Thomas Hutchinson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Oliver" title="Andrew Oliver">Andrew Oliver</a>, governor and lieutenant governor of the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Massachusetts_Bay" title="Province of Massachusetts Bay">Province of Massachusetts Bay</a>, proving that they had encouraged the Crown to crack down on Bostonians. Franklin sent them to America, where they escalated the tensions. The letters were finally <a href="/wiki/News_leak" title="News leak">leaked</a> to the public in the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Gazette" title="Boston Gazette">Boston Gazette</a></i> in mid-June 1773,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> causing a political firestorm in Massachusetts and raising significant questions in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penegar29_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penegar29-130">[130]</a></sup> The British began to regard him as the fomenter of serious trouble. Hopes for a peaceful solution ended as he was systematically ridiculed and humiliated by <a href="/wiki/Solicitor_General_for_England_and_Wales" title="Solicitor General for England and Wales">Solicitor-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Wedderburn,_1st_Earl_of_Rosslyn" title="Alexander Wedderburn, 1st Earl of Rosslyn">Alexander Wedderburn</a>, before the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">Privy Council</a> on January 29, 1774. He returned to Philadelphia in March 1775, and abandoned his accommodationist stance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coming_of_revolution">Coming of revolution</span></h3>
<p>In 1763, soon after Franklin returned to Pennsylvania from England for the first time, the western frontier was engulfed in a bitter war known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontiac%27s_Rebellion" title="Pontiac's Rebellion">Pontiac's Rebellion</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Paxton_Boys" title="Paxton Boys">Paxton Boys</a>, a group of settlers convinced that the Pennsylvania government was not doing enough to protect them from <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">American Indian</a> raids, murdered a group of peaceful <a href="/wiki/Susquehannock" title="Susquehannock">Susquehannock</a> Indians and marched on Philadelphia. Franklin helped to organize a local <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militia</a> to defend the capital against the mob. He met with the Paxton leaders and persuaded them to disperse. Franklin wrote a scathing attack against the <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racial prejudice</a> of the Paxton Boys. "If an <i>Indian</i> injures me", he asked, "does it follow that I may revenge that Injury on all <i>Indians</i>?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>He provided an early response to British surveillance through his own network of <a href="/wiki/Surveillance_art" title="Surveillance art">counter-surveillance and manipulation</a>. "He waged a public relations campaign, secured secret aid, played a role in privateering expeditions, and churned out effective and inflammatory propaganda."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Declaration_of_Independence">Declaration of Independence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_Independence_(1819),_by_John_Trumbull.jpg"><img alt="About 50 men, most of them seated, are in a large meeting room. Most are focused on the five men standing in the center of the room. The tallest of the five is laying a document on a table." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1969" data-file-width="2976" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/260px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/390px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/520px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_Independence_(1819),_by_John_Trumbull.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a> depicts the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">Committee of Five</a> presenting their work to the Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>By the time Franklin arrived in Philadelphia on May 5, 1775, after his second mission to Great Britain, the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> had begun—with fighting between colonials and British at <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord" title="Battles of Lexington and Concord">Lexington and Concord</a>. The New England militia had trapped the main British army in Boston. The Pennsylvania Assembly unanimously chose Franklin as their delegate to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a>. In June 1776, he was appointed a member of the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">Committee of Five</a> that drafted the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>. Although he was temporarily disabled by <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a> and unable to attend most meetings of the Committee, Franklin made several "small but important"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> changes to the draft sent to him by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>.
</p><p>At the signing, he is quoted as having replied to a comment by <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock" title="John Hancock">John Hancock</a> that they must all hang together: "Yes, we must, indeed, all hang together, or most assuredly we shall all hang separately."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postmaster">Postmaster</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_stamp_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="346" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7e/Franklin_stamp_2013.jpg/260px-Franklin_stamp_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7e/Franklin_stamp_2013.jpg 1.5x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_stamp_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Benjamin Franklin on a <a href="/wiki/Canada_Post" title="Canada Post">Canada Post</a> stamp of 2013, with colonial <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a> in background</div></div></div>
<p>Well known as a printer and publisher, Franklin was appointed postmaster of Philadelphia in 1737, holding the office until 1753, when he and publisher <a href="/wiki/William_Hunter_(publisher)" title="William Hunter (publisher)">William Hunter</a> were named deputy postmasters–general of British North America, the first to hold the office. (<a href="/wiki/Postmaster_General_of_the_United_Kingdom#Two_Postmasters_General" title="Postmaster General of the United Kingdom">Joint appointments</a> were standard at the time, for political reasons.) Franklin was responsible for the British colonies from Pennsylvania north and east, as far as the <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">island of Newfoundland</a>. A post office for local and outgoing mail had been established in <a href="/wiki/Halifax,_Nova_Scotia" title="Halifax, Nova Scotia">Halifax, Nova Scotia</a>, by local stationer Benjamin Leigh, on April 23, 1754, but service was irregular. Franklin opened the first post office to offer regular, monthly mail in what would later become Canada, at Halifax, on December 9, 1755. Meantime, Hunter became postal administrator in <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Williamsburg" title="Colonial Williamsburg">Williamsburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> and oversaw areas south of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annapolis" title="Annapolis">Annapolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>. Franklin reorganized the service's accounting system, then improved speed of delivery between Philadelphia, New York and Boston. By 1761, efficiencies led to the first profits for the colonial post office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="411" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg/170px-Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg/255px-Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg/340px-Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_SC1_1847.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First issue of Benjamin Franklin on US <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin#Franklin_on_U.S._Postage" title="Benjamin Franklin">postage stamp</a>, issue of 1847</div></div></div>
<p>When the lands of <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a> were ceded to the British under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris</a> in 1763, the new British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763%E2%80%9391)" title="Province of Quebec (1763–91)">province of Quebec</a> was created among them, and Franklin saw mail service expanded between <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trois-Rivi%C3%A8res" title="Trois-Rivières">Trois-Rivières</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a>, and New York. For the greater part of his appointment, Franklin lived in England (from 1757 to 1762, and again from 1764 to 1774)—about three-quarters of his term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Eventually, his sympathies for the rebel cause in the American Revolution led to his dismissal on January 31, 1774.
</p><p>On July 26, 1775, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> established the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Post Office</a> and named Benjamin Franklin as the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">United States Postmaster General</a>. Franklin had been a postmaster for decades and was a natural choice for the position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> He had just returned from England and was appointed chairman of a Committee of Investigation to establish a postal system. The report of the Committee, providing for the appointment of a postmaster general for the 13 American colonies, was considered by the Continental Congress on July 25 and 26. On July 26, 1775, Franklin was appointed Postmaster General, the first appointed under the Continental Congress. It established a postal system that became the United States Post Office, a system that continues to operate today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Ambassador_to_France"></span>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ambassador_to_France:_1776.E2.80.931785"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ambassador_to_France:_1776–1785">Ambassador to France: 1776–1785</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin1877.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1968" data-file-width="1733" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Franklin1877.jpg/170px-Franklin1877.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Franklin1877.jpg/255px-Franklin1877.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Franklin1877.jpg/340px-Franklin1877.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin1877.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin, in his <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">fur</a> hat, charmed the French with what they perceived as rustic New World <a href="/wiki/Genius" title="Genius">genius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lightning_group=Note_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-lightning_group=Note-141">[141]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In December 1776, Franklin was dispatched to France as <a href="/wiki/Commissioner" title="Commissioner">commissioner</a> for the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> He took with him as secretary his 16-year-old grandson, <a href="/wiki/William_Temple_Franklin" title="William Temple Franklin">William Temple Franklin</a>. They lived in a home in the Parisian suburb of <a href="/wiki/Passy" title="Passy">Passy</a>, donated by <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Donatien_Le_Ray_de_Chaumont" title="Jacques-Donatien Le Ray de Chaumont">Jacques-Donatien Le Ray de Chaumont</a>, who supported the United States. Franklin remained in France until 1785. He conducted the affairs of his country toward the French nation with great success, which included securing a critical military alliance in 1778 and negotiating the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris (1783)</a>.
</p><p>Among his associates in France was <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Gabriel_Riqueti,_comte_de_Mirabeau" title="Honoré Gabriel Riqueti, comte de Mirabeau">Honoré Gabriel Riqueti, comte de Mirabeau</a>—a French Revolutionary writer, orator and statesman who in early 1791 would be elected president of the <a href="/wiki/National_Constituent_Assembly_(France)" title="National Constituent Assembly (France)">National Assembly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> In July 1784, Franklin met with Mirabeau and contributed anonymous materials that the Frenchman used in his first signed work: <i>Considerations sur l'ordre de Cincinnatus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The publication was critical of the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Cincinnati" title="Society of the Cincinnati">Society of the Cincinnati</a>, established in the United States. Franklin and Mirabeau thought of it as a "noble order", inconsistent with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egalitarian" title="Egalitarian">egalitarian</a> ideals of the new republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his stay in France, Benjamin Franklin was active as a <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemason</a>, serving as Venerable Master of the Lodge <a href="/wiki/Les_Neuf_S%C5%93urs" title="Les Neuf Sœurs">Les Neuf Sœurs</a> from 1779 until 1781. He was the 106th member of the Lodge. In 1784, when <a href="/wiki/Franz_Mesmer" title="Franz Mesmer">Franz Mesmer</a> began to publicize his theory of "<a href="/wiki/Animal_magnetism" title="Animal magnetism">animal magnetism</a>" which was considered offensive by many, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI</a> appointed a commission to investigate it. These included the chemist <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a>, the physician <a href="/wiki/Joseph-Ignace_Guillotin" title="Joseph-Ignace Guillotin">Joseph-Ignace Guillotin</a>, the astronomer <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sylvain_Bailly" title="Jean Sylvain Bailly">Jean Sylvain Bailly</a>, and Benjamin Franklin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> In 1781, he was elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAAS_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="3000" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg/220px-Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg/330px-Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg/440px-Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Libertas_Americana_silver_medallion_1783.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>While in France Franklin designed and commissioned <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Dupr%C3%A9" title="Augustin Dupré">Augustin Dupré</a> to engrave the medallion <i>"Libertas Americana"</i> minted in Paris in 1783.</div></div></div>
<p>Franklin's advocacy for religious tolerance in France contributed to arguments made by French philosophers and politicians that resulted in <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI</a>'s signing of the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Versailles" title="Edict of Versailles">Edict of Versailles</a> in November 1787. This edict effectively nullified the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Fontainebleau" title="Edict of Fontainebleau">Edict of Fontainebleau</a>, which had denied non-Catholics civil status and the right to openly practice their faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin also served as American minister to Sweden, although he never visited that country.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He negotiated a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Amity_and_Commerce_(United_States%E2%80%93Sweden)" title="Treaty of Amity and Commerce (United States–Sweden)">treaty</a> that was signed in April 1783. On August 27, 1783, in Paris, Franklin witnessed the world's first hydrogen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balloon_(aircraft)" title="Balloon (aircraft)">balloon</a> flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcceF_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-EcceF-149">[149]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Robert_brothers#First_hydrogen_balloon" title="Robert brothers">Le Globe</a></i>, created by professor <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Charles" title="Jacques Charles">Jacques Charles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_brothers" title="Robert brothers">Les Frères Robert</a>, was watched by a vast crowd as it rose from the <a href="/wiki/Champ_de_Mars" title="Champ de Mars">Champ de Mars</a> (now the site of the <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">Eiffel Tower</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sci&amp;Soc_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sci&amp;Soc-150">[150]</a></sup> Franklin became so enthusiastic that he subscribed financially to the next project to build a manned hydrogen balloon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fid_Green_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fid_Green-151">[151]</a></sup> On December 1, 1783, Franklin was seated in the special enclosure for honoured guests when <i><a href="/wiki/Robert_brothers#First_manned_hydrogen_balloon_flight" title="Robert brothers">La Charlière</a></i> took off from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jardin_des_Tuileries" title="Jardin des Tuileries">Jardin des Tuileries</a>, piloted by Jacques Charles and <a href="/wiki/Robert_brothers" title="Robert brothers">Nicolas-Louis Robert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcceF_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-EcceF-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAI_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAI-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_Convention">Constitutional Convention</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2600" data-file-width="3632" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg/220px-Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg/330px-Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg/440px-Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin%27s_return_to_Philadelphia_1785_cph.3g09906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Franklin's return to Philadelphia, 1785</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Leon_Gerome_Ferris" title="Jean Leon Gerome Ferris">Jean Leon Gerome Ferris</a></div></div></div>
<p>When he returned home in 1785, Franklin occupied a position only second to that of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> as the champion of American independence. Le Ray honored him with a commissioned portrait painted by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Duplessis" title="Joseph Duplessis">Joseph Duplessis</a>, which now hangs in the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">National Portrait Gallery</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> in Washington, D.C. After his return, Franklin became an <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">abolitionist</a> and freed his two slaves. He eventually became president of the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Abolition_Society" title="Pennsylvania Abolition Society">Pennsylvania Abolition Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1787, Franklin served as a delegate to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" title="Philadelphia Convention">Philadelphia Convention</a>. He held an honorary position and seldom engaged in debate. He is the only Founding Father who is a signatory of all four of the major documents of the founding of the United States: the Declaration of Independence, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alliance_(1778)" title="Treaty of Alliance (1778)">Treaty of Alliance</a> with France, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>.
</p><p>In 1787, a group of prominent ministers in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster, Pennsylvania">Lancaster, Pennsylvania</a>, proposed the foundation of a new college named in Franklin's honor. Franklin donated £200 towards the development of Franklin College (now called <a href="/wiki/Franklin_%26_Marshall_College" title="Franklin &amp; Marshall College">Franklin &amp; Marshall College</a>).
</p><p>Between 1771 and 1788, he finished his <a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Benjamin_Franklin" title="The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin">autobiography</a>. While it was at first addressed to his son, it was later completed for the benefit of mankind at the request of a friend.
</p><p>Franklin strongly supported the right to <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In those wretched countries where a man cannot call his tongue his own, he can scarce call anything his own. Whoever would overthrow the liberty of a nation must begin by subduing the freeness of speech ...
Without freedom of thought there can be no such thing as wisdom, and no such thing as public liberty without freedom of speech, which is the right of every man ...</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Silence_Dogood" title="Silence Dogood">Silence Dogood</a> no. 8, 1722<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President_of_Pennsylvania">President of Pennsylvania</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FRANKLIN,_Benjamin_(signed_check).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1833" data-file-width="4500" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/FRANKLIN%2C_Benjamin_%28signed_check%29.jpg/220px-FRANKLIN%2C_Benjamin_%28signed_check%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/FRANKLIN%2C_Benjamin_%28signed_check%29.jpg/330px-FRANKLIN%2C_Benjamin_%28signed_check%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/FRANKLIN%2C_Benjamin_%28signed_check%29.jpg/440px-FRANKLIN%2C_Benjamin_%28signed_check%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FRANKLIN,_Benjamin_(signed_check).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin autograph check signed during his Presidency of Pennsylvania</div></div></div>
<p>Special balloting conducted October 18, 1785, unanimously elected Franklin the sixth <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Executive_Council_of_the_Commonwealth_of_Pennsylvania#Presidents_of_Council" title="Supreme Executive Council of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania">president</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Executive_Council_of_the_Commonwealth_of_Pennsylvania" title="Supreme Executive Council of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania">Supreme Executive Council of Pennsylvania</a>, replacing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Dickinson_(Pennsylvania_and_Delaware)" title="John Dickinson (Pennsylvania and Delaware)">John Dickinson</a>. The office was practically that of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Pennsylvania" title="List of Governors of Pennsylvania">governor</a>. Franklin held that office for slightly over three years, longer than any other, and served the constitutional limit of three full terms. Shortly after his initial election he was reelected to a full term on October 29, 1785, and again in the fall of 1786 and on October 31, 1787. In that capacity he served as host to the Constitutional Convention of 1787 in Philadelphia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Virtue.2C_religion.2C_and_personal_beliefs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Virtue,_religion,_and_personal_beliefs">Virtue, religion, and personal beliefs</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_(1706%E2%80%931790)_MET_DT2883.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1861" data-file-width="1454" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Benjamin_Franklin_%281706%E2%80%931790%29_MET_DT2883.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Franklin_%281706%E2%80%931790%29_MET_DT2883.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Benjamin_Franklin_%281706%E2%80%931790%29_MET_DT2883.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Franklin_%281706%E2%80%931790%29_MET_DT2883.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Benjamin_Franklin_%281706%E2%80%931790%29_MET_DT2883.jpg/340px-Benjamin_Franklin_%281706%E2%80%931790%29_MET_DT2883.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_(1706%E2%80%931790)_MET_DT2883.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bust of Franklin by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Antoine_Houdon" title="Jean-Antoine Houdon">Jean-Antoine Houdon</a></div></div></div>
<p>Like the other advocates of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a>, Franklin emphasized that the new republic could survive only if the people were virtuous. All his life he explored the role of civic and personal virtue, as expressed in <i>Poor Richard's</i> <a href="/wiki/Aphorism" title="Aphorism">aphorisms</a>. Franklin felt that organized religion was necessary to keep men good to their fellow men, but rarely attended religious services himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> When Franklin met <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> in Paris and asked his fellow member of the Enlightenment vanguard to bless his grandson, Voltaire said in English, "God and Liberty", and added, "this is the only appropriate benediction for the grandson of Monsieur Franklin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="640" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg/260px-Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg/390px-Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg/520px-Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico_-_Voltaire_aben%C3%A7oando_o_neto_de_Franklin_em_nome_de_Deus_e_da_Liberdade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Voltaire blessing Franklin's grandson, in the name of God and Liberty</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Am%C3%A9rico" title="Pedro Américo">Pedro Américo</a></div></div></div>
<p>Franklin's parents were both pious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The family attended the <a href="/wiki/Old_South_Church" title="Old South Church">Old South Church</a>, the most liberal Puritan congregation in Boston, where Benjamin Franklin was baptized in 1706.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Franklin's father, a poor <a href="/wiki/Chandlery" title="Chandlery">chandler</a>, owned a copy of a book, <i>Bonifacius: Essays to Do Good</i>, by the Puritan preacher and family friend <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Mather" title="Cotton Mather">Cotton Mather</a>, which Franklin often cited as a key influence on his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Franklin's first pen name, Silence Dogood, paid homage both to the book and to a widely known sermon by Mather. The book preached the importance of forming voluntary associations to benefit society. Franklin learned about forming do-good associations from Cotton Mather, but his organizational skills made him the most influential force in making voluntarism an enduring part of the American ethos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin formulated a presentation of his beliefs and published it in 1728.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> It did not mention many of the Puritan ideas regarding salvation, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divinity_of_Jesus" title="Divinity of Jesus">divinity of Jesus</a>, or indeed much religious dogma. He clarified himself as a <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a> in his 1771 autobiography,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> although he still considered himself a Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christian_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christian-164">[164]</a></sup> He retained a strong faith in a God as the wellspring of morality and goodness in man, and as a Providential actor in history responsible for American independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_by_Hiram_Powers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="230" height="440" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Benjamin_Franklin_by_Hiram_Powers.jpg/220px-Benjamin_Franklin_by_Hiram_Powers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/Benjamin_Franklin_by_Hiram_Powers.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_by_Hiram_Powers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Benjamin Franklin by Hiram Powers</div></div></div>
<p>It was Ben Franklin who, at a critical impasse during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" title="Philadelphia Convention">Constitutional Convention</a> in June 1787, attempted to introduce the practice of daily common prayer with these words:
</p>
<blockquote><p>... In the beginning of the contest with G. Britain, when we were sensible of danger we had daily prayer in this room for the Divine Protection. Our prayers, Sir, were heard, and they were graciously answered. All of us who were engaged in the struggle must have observed frequent instances of a Superintending providence in our favor. ... And have we now forgotten that powerful friend? or do we imagine that we no longer need His assistance. I have lived, Sir, a long time and the longer I live, the more convincing proofs I see of this truth—that God governs in the affairs of men. And if a sparrow cannot fall to the ground without his notice, is it probable that an empire can rise without his aid? We have been assured, Sir, in the sacred writings that "except the Lord build they labor in vain that build it." I firmly believe this; and I also believe that without his concurring aid we shall succeed in this political building no better than the Builders of Babel: ... I therefore beg leave to move—that henceforth prayers imploring the assistance of Heaven, and its blessings on our deliberations, be held in this Assembly every morning before we proceed to business, and that one or more of the Clergy of this City be requested to officiate in that service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The motion met with resistance and was never brought to a vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Franklin was an enthusiastic supporter of the evangelical minister <a href="/wiki/George_Whitefield" title="George Whitefield">George Whitefield</a> during the <a href="/wiki/First_Great_Awakening" title="First Great Awakening">First Great Awakening</a>. Franklin did not subscribe to Whitefield's theology, but he admired Whitefield for exhorting people to worship God through good works. Franklin published all of Whitefield's sermons and journals, thereby earning a lot of money and boosting the Great Awakening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>When he stopped attending church, Franklin wrote in his autobiography:
</p>
<blockquote><p>... Sunday being my studying day, I never was without some religious principles. I never doubted, for instance, the existence of the Deity; that He made the world, and governed it by His providence; that the most acceptable service of God was the doing good to man; that our souls are immortal; and that all crime will be punished, and virtue rewarded, either here or hereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Franklin retained a lifelong commitment to the Puritan virtues and political values he had grown up with, and through his civic work and publishing, he succeeded in passing these values into the American culture permanently. He had a "passion for virtue".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> These Puritan values included his devotion to egalitarianism, education, industry, thrift, honesty, temperance, charity and community spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>The classical authors read in the Enlightenment period taught an abstract <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">ideal of republican government</a> based on hierarchical social orders of king, aristocracy and commoners. It was widely believed that English liberties relied on their balance of power, but also hierarchal deference to the privileged class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> "Puritanism ... and the epidemic evangelism of the mid-eighteenth century, had created challenges to the traditional notions of social stratification"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailyn303_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailyn303-174">[174]</a></sup> by preaching that the Bible taught all men are equal, that the true value of a man lies in his moral behavior, not his class, and that all men can be saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailyn303_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bailyn303-174">[174]</a></sup> Franklin, steeped in Puritanism and an enthusiastic supporter of the evangelical movement, rejected the salvation dogma, but embraced the radical notion of egalitarian democracy.
</p><p>Franklin's commitment to teach these values was itself something he gained from his Puritan upbringing, with its stress on "inculcating virtue and character in themselves and their communities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> These Puritan values and the desire to pass them on, were one of Franklin's quintessentially American characteristics, and helped shape the character of the nation. Franklin's writings on <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a> were derided by some European authors, such as Jackob Fugger in his critical work <i>Portrait of American Culture</i>. <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> considered Franklin's ethical writings a culmination of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">Protestant ethic</a>, which ethic created the social conditions necessary for the birth of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Franklin's notable characteristics was his respect, tolerance and promotion of all churches. Referring to his experience in Philadelphia, he wrote in <a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Benjamin_Franklin" title="The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin">his autobiography</a>, "new Places of worship were continually wanted, and generally erected by voluntary Contribution, my Mite for such purpose, whatever might be the Sect, was never refused."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-169">[169]</a></sup> "He helped create a new type of nation that would draw strength from its religious pluralism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaacson_2003_p_93ff_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isaacson_2003_p_93ff-177">[177]</a></sup> The evangelical revivalists who were active mid-century, such as Franklin's friend and preacher, George Whitefield, were the greatest advocates of religious freedom, "claiming liberty of conscience to be an 'inalienable right of every rational creature.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Whitefield's supporters in Philadelphia, including Franklin, erected "a large, new hall, that ... could provide a pulpit to anyone of any belief."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Franklin's rejection of dogma and doctrine and his stress on the God of ethics and morality and <a href="/wiki/Civic_virtue" title="Civic virtue">civic virtue</a> made him the "prophet of tolerance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaacson_2003_p_93ff_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Isaacson_2003_p_93ff-177">[177]</a></sup> Franklin composed "A Parable Against Persecution", an apocryphal 51st chapter of Genesis in which God teaches Abraham the duty of tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> While he was living in London in 1774, he was present at the birth of <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_Unitarian_and_Free_Christian_Churches" title="General Assembly of Unitarian and Free Christian Churches">British Unitarianism</a>, attending the inaugural session of the <a href="/wiki/Essex_Street_Chapel" title="Essex Street Chapel">Essex Street Chapel</a>, at which <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Lindsey" title="Theophilus Lindsey">Theophilus Lindsey</a> drew together the first avowedly <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> congregation in England; this was somewhat politically risky, and pushed religious tolerance to new boundaries, as a denial of the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> was illegal until <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_Trinity_Act_1813" title="Doctrine of the Trinity Act 1813">the 1813 Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr_Richard_Price,_DD,_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2958" data-file-width="2268" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dr_Richard_Price%2C_DD%2C_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg/220px-Dr_Richard_Price%2C_DD%2C_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dr_Richard_Price%2C_DD%2C_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg/330px-Dr_Richard_Price%2C_DD%2C_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dr_Richard_Price%2C_DD%2C_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg/440px-Dr_Richard_Price%2C_DD%2C_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr_Richard_Price,_DD,_FRS_-_Benjamin_West.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_Price" title="Richard Price">Dr Richard Price</a>, the radical minister of <a href="/wiki/Newington_Green_Unitarian_Church" title="Newington Green Unitarian Church">Newington Green Unitarian Church</a>, holding a letter from Franklin</div></div></div>
