<p>Although Franklin's parents had intended for him to have a career in the Church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autobio_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-autobio-13">[13]</a></sup> Franklin as a young man adopted the Enlightenment religious belief in <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>, that God's truths can be found entirely through nature and reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> "I soon became a thorough Deist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> As a young man he rejected Christian dogma in a 1725 pamphlet <i><a href="/wiki/A_Dissertation_on_Liberty_and_Necessity,_Pleasure_and_Pain" title="A Dissertation on Liberty and Necessity, Pleasure and Pain">A Dissertation on Liberty and Necessity, Pleasure and Pain</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> which he later saw as an embarrassment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaacson45_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isaacson45-185">[185]</a></sup> while simultaneously asserting that God is "all wise, <a href="/wiki/Omnibenevolence" title="Omnibenevolence">all good</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">all powerful</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isaacson45_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Isaacson45-185">[185]</a></sup> He defended his rejection of religious dogma with these words: "I think opinions should be judged by their influences and effects; and if a man holds none that tend to make him less virtuous or more vicious, it may be concluded that he holds none that are dangerous, which I hope is the case with me." After the disillusioning experience of seeing the decay in his own moral standards, and those of two friends in London whom he had converted to Deism, Franklin turned back to a belief in the importance of organized religion, on the pragmatic grounds that without God and organized churches, man will not be good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> Moreover, because of his proposal that <a href="/wiki/Christian_prayer" title="Christian prayer">prayers</a> be said in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_of_1787" title="Constitutional Convention of 1787">Constitutional Convention of 1787</a>, many have contended that in his later life Franklin became a <a href="/wiki/Piety" title="Piety">pious</a> Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pious_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pious-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faith_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faith-188">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to David Morgan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Franklin was a proponent of religion in general. He prayed to "Powerful Goodness" and referred to God as "the infinite". <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> noted that Franklin was a mirror in which people saw their own religion: "The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholics</a> thought him almost a Catholic. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> claimed him as one of them. The <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterians</a> thought him half a Presbyterian, and the <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Friends</a> believed him a wet Quaker." Whatever else Franklin was, concludes Morgan, "he was a true champion of generic religion." In a letter to Richard Price, Franklin stated that he believed that religion should support itself without help from the government, claiming, "When a Religion is good, I conceive that it will support itself; and, when it cannot support itself, and God does not take care to support, so that its Professors are oblig'd to call for the help of the Civil Power, it is a sign, I apprehend, of its being a bad one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1790, just about a month before he died, Franklin wrote a letter to <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles" title="Ezra Stiles">Ezra Stiles</a>, president of <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>, who had asked him his views on religion:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_of_Nazareth" title="Jesus of Nazareth">Jesus of Nazareth</a>, my Opinion of whom you particularly desire, I think the System of Morals and his Religion, as he left them to us, the best the world ever saw or is likely to see; but I apprehend it has received various corrupt changes, and I have, with most of the present <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">Dissenters in England</a>, some Doubts as to his divinity; tho' it is a question I do not dogmatize upon, having never studied it, and I think it needless to busy myself with it now, when I expect soon an Opportunity of knowing the Truth with less Trouble. I see no harm, however, in its being believed, if that belief has the good consequence, as it probably has, of making his doctrines more respected and better observed; especially as I do not perceive that the Supreme takes it amiss, by distinguishing the unbelievers in his government of the world with any particular marks of his displeasure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vandoren_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-vandoren-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>On July 4, 1776, Congress appointed a three-member committee composed of Franklin, Thomas Jefferson, and John Adams to design the <a href="/wiki/Great_Seal_of_the_United_States" title="Great Seal of the United States">Great Seal of the United States</a>. Franklin's proposal (which was not adopted) featured the motto: "Rebellion to Tyrants is Obedience to God" and a scene from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pillar_of_Fire_(theophany)" title="Pillar of Fire (theophany)">pillar of fire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a> depicted as <a href="/wiki/Pharaohs_in_the_Bible#Pharaoh_of_the_Exodus" title="Pharaohs in the Bible">pharaoh</a>. The design that was produced was never acted upon by Congress, and the Great Seal's design was not finalized until a third committee was appointed in 1782.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thirteen_Virtues">Thirteen Virtues</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG/220px-Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG/330px-Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG/440px-Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklin_bust_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0924.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin bust in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archives" title="Archives">Archives</a> Department of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> in New York City</div></div></div>
<p>Franklin sought to cultivate his character by a plan of 13 virtues, which he developed at age 20 (in 1726) and continued to practice in some form for the rest of his life. His <a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Benjamin_Franklin" title="The Autobiography of Benjamin Franklin">autobiography</a> lists his 13 virtues as:
</p>
<ol><li>"<a href="/wiki/Temperance_(virtue)" title="Temperance (virtue)">Temperance</a>. Eat not to dullness; drink not to elevation."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Silence" title="Silence">Silence</a>. Speak not but what may benefit others or yourself; avoid trifling conversation."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Order_(virtue)" title="Order (virtue)">Order</a>. Let all your things have their places; let each part of your business have its time."</li>
<li>"Resolution. Resolve to perform what you ought; perform without fail what you resolve."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Frugality" title="Frugality">Frugality</a>. Make no expense but to do good to others or yourself; i.e., waste nothing."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">Industry</a>. Lose no time; be always employ'd in something useful; cut off all unnecessary actions."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Sincerity" title="Sincerity">Sincerity</a>. Use no hurtful deceit; think innocently and justly, and, if you speak, speak accordingly."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">Justice</a>. Wrong none by doing injuries, or omitting the benefits that are your duty."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Moderation" title="Moderation">Moderation</a>. Avoid extremes; forbear resenting injuries so much as you think they deserve."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Cleanliness" title="Cleanliness">Cleanliness</a>. Tolerate no uncleanliness in body, clothes, or habitation."</li>
<li>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tranquility" title="Tranquility">Tranquility</a>. Be not disturbed at trifles, or at accidents common or unavoidable."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Chastity" title="Chastity">Chastity</a>. Rarely use <a href="/wiki/Human_sexuality" title="Human sexuality">venery</a> but for health or offspring, never to dullness, weakness, or the injury of your own or another's peace or reputation."</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Humility" title="Humility">Humility</a>. Imitate Jesus and <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>."</li></ol>
<p>Franklin did not try to work on them all at once. Instead, he would work on one and only one each week "leaving all others to their ordinary chance." While Franklin did not live completely by his virtues, and by his own admission he fell short of them many times, he believed the attempt made him a better man contributing greatly to his success and happiness, which is why in his autobiography, he devoted more pages to this plan than to any other single point; in his autobiography Franklin wrote, "I hope, therefore, that some of my descendants may follow the example and reap the benefit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span></h2>
<p>Franklin owned as many as seven slaves, two males who worked in his household and his shop. Franklin posted paid ads for the sale of slaves and for the capture of runaway slaves and allowed the sale of slaves in his general store. Franklin profited from both the international and domestic slave trade, even criticizing slaves who had run off to join the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> during the colonial wars of the 1740s and 1750s. Franklin, however, later became a "cautious abolitionist" and became an outspoken critic of landed gentry slavery. In 1758, Franklin advocated the opening of a school for the education of black slaves in Philadelphia. Franklin took two slaves to England with him, Peter and King, and King left his service there in 1756: by 1758 he was working for "a lady in Suffolk".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Whether Franklin could have compelled King's return is open to doubt in the light of earlier English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_Law" title="Common Law">Common Law</a> decisions and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_at_common_law#Shanley_v_Harvey" title="Slavery at common law">the subsequent case of Shanley v Harvey</a>, but in fact he did not do so.
</p><p>After returning from England in 1762, Franklin became more anti-slavery. By 1770, Franklin had freed his slaves and attacked the system of slavery and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_slave_trade" title="International slave trade">international slave trade</a>. Franklin, however, refused to publicly debate the issue of slavery at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1787_Constitutional_Convention" title="1787 Constitutional Convention">1787 Constitutional Convention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> Franklin tended to take both sides of the issue of slavery, never fully divesting himself from the institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his later years, as Congress was forced to deal with the issue of slavery, Franklin wrote several essays that stressed the importance of the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolition of slavery</a> and of the integration of blacks into American society. These writings included:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/An_Address_to_the_Public" title="s:An Address to the Public">An Address to the Public</a></i> (1789)</li>
<li><i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/A_Plan_for_Improving_the_Condition_of_the_Free_Blacks" title="s:A Plan for Improving the Condition of the Free Blacks">A Plan for Improving the Condition of the Free Blacks</a></i> (1789)</li>
<li><i>Sidi Mehemet Ibrahim on the Slave Trade</i> (1790)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In 1790, <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a> from New York and Pennsylvania presented their petition for abolition to Congress. Their argument against slavery was backed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Abolitionist_Society" title="Pennsylvania Abolitionist Society">Pennsylvania Abolitionist Society</a> and its president, Benjamin Franklin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG/220px-Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG/330px-Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG/440px-Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philly_2010_transit_071.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The grave of Benjamin Franklin, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania</div></div></div>
<p>Franklin suffered from obesity throughout his middle-aged and later years, which resulted in multiple health problems, particularly <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a>, which worsened as he aged. In poor health during the signing of the US Constitution in 1787, he was rarely seen in public from then until his death.
</p><p>Benjamin Franklin died from <a href="/wiki/Pleurisy" title="Pleurisy">pleuritic attack</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> at his home in Philadelphia on April 17, 1790, at age 84. His death is described in the book <i>The Life of Benjamin Franklin</i>, quoting from the account of Dr. John Jones:
</p>
<blockquote><p>... when the pain and difficulty of breathing entirely left him, and his family were flattering themselves with the hopes of his recovery, when an imposthume, which had formed itself in his lungs, suddenly burst, and discharged a quantity of matter, which he continued to throw up while he had power; but, as that failed, the organs of respiration became gradually oppressed; a calm, lethargic state succeeded; and on the 17th instant (April 1790), about eleven o'clock at night, he quietly expired, closing a long and useful life of eighty-four years and three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Approximately 20,000 people attended his funeral. He was interred in <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Burial_Ground" title="Christ Church Burial Ground">Christ Church Burial Ground</a> in Philadelphia. In 1728, aged 22, Franklin wrote what he hoped would be his own epitaph:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The Body of B. Franklin Printer; Like the Cover of an old Book, Its Contents torn out, And stript of its Lettering and Gilding, Lies here, Food for Worms. But the Work shall not be wholly lost: For it will, as he believ'd, appear once more, In a new &amp; more perfect Edition, Corrected and Amended By the Author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Franklin's actual grave, however, as he specified in his final will, simply reads "Benjamin and Deborah Franklin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Obverse_of_the_series_2009_$100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="1200" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg/260px-Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg/390px-Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg/520px-Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Obverse_of_the_series_2009_$100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franklin on the Series 2009 <a href="/wiki/United_States_one_hundred-dollar_bill" title="United States one hundred-dollar bill">hundred dollar bill</a></div></div></div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 230px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><caption>External video</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.c-span.org/video/?409189-1/benjamin-franklins-legacy" rel="nofollow">Presentation by Franklin biographer Walter Isaacson to the New York Historical Society on Benjamin Franklin's legacy, May 11, 2016</a>, <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.c-span.org/video/?190532-1/benjamin-franklin-search-better-world" rel="nofollow">Panel discussion with Ellen R. Cohn, Emma J. Lapsansky-Werner, J.A. Leo Lemay, Billy Gordon Smith, James Srodes, and Page Talbott on <i>Benjamin Franklin: In Search of a Better World</i>, November 30, 2005</a>, <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg/220px-Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg/330px-Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg/440px-Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marble memorial statue, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial" title="Benjamin Franklin National Memorial">Benjamin Franklin National Memorial</a></div></div></div><p>A signer of both the Declaration of Independence and the Constitution, Franklin is considered one of the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a>. His pervasive influence in the early history of the nation has led to his being jocularly called "the only President of the United States who was never President of the United States."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Franklin's likeness is ubiquitous. Since 1928, it has adorned American <a href="/wiki/United_States_one_hundred-dollar_bill" title="United States one hundred-dollar bill">$100 bills</a>, which are sometimes referred to in slang as "Benjamins" or "Franklins." From 1948 to 1963, Franklin's portrait was on the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_half_dollar" title="Franklin half dollar">half dollar</a>. He has appeared on a <a href="/wiki/United_States_fifty-dollar_bill" title="United States fifty-dollar bill">$50 bill</a> and on several varieties of the $100 bill from 1914 and 1918. Franklin appears on the $1,000 Series EE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasury_security#Savings_bond" title="Treasury security">Savings bond</a>. Philadelphia's <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Parkway" title="Benjamin Franklin Parkway">Benjamin Franklin Parkway</a> (a major thoroughfare) and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Bridge" title="Benjamin Franklin Bridge">Benjamin Franklin Bridge</a> (the first major bridge to connect Philadelphia with New Jersey) are named in his honor.
</p><p>In 1976, as part of a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">bicentennial</a> celebration, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> dedicated a 20-foot (6 m) marble statue in Philadelphia's <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Institute" title="Franklin Institute">Franklin Institute</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_National_Memorial" title="Benjamin Franklin National Memorial">Benjamin Franklin National Memorial</a>. Many of Franklin's personal possessions are also on display at the Institute, one of the few national memorials located on <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">private property</a>.
</p><p>In London, his house at 36 Craven Street, which is the only surviving former residence of Benjamin Franklin, was first marked with a <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> and has since been opened to the public as the <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_House" title="Benjamin Franklin House">Benjamin Franklin House</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> In 1998, workmen restoring the building dug up the remains of six children and four adults hidden below the home. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> reported on February 11, 1998:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Initial estimates are that the bones are about 200 years old and were buried at the time Franklin was living in the house, which was his home from 1757 to 1762 and from 1764 to 1775. Most of the bones show signs of having been dissected, sawn or cut. One skull has been drilled with several holes. Paul Knapman, the Westminster Coroner, said yesterday: "I cannot totally discount the possibility of a crime. There is still a possibility that I may have to hold an inquest.</p></blockquote>
<p>The Friends of Benjamin Franklin House (the organization responsible for the restoration) note that the bones were likely placed there by <a href="/wiki/William_Hewson_(surgeon)" title="William Hewson (surgeon)">William Hewson</a>, who lived in the house for two years and who had built a small anatomy school at the back of the house. They note that while Franklin likely knew what Hewson was doing, he probably did not participate in any dissections because he was much more of a physicist than a medical man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bequest">Bequest</span></h3>
<p>Franklin <a href="/wiki/Bequest" title="Bequest">bequeathed</a> £1,000 (about $4,400 at the time, or about $112,000 in 2011 dollars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>) each to the cities of Boston and Philadelphia, in trust to gather interest for 200 years. The trust began in 1785 when the French mathematician <a href="/wiki/Charles-Joseph_Mathon_de_la_Cour" title="Charles-Joseph Mathon de la Cour">Charles-Joseph Mathon de la Cour</a>, who admired Franklin greatly, wrote a friendly <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">parody</a> of Franklin's "Poor Richard's Almanack" called "Fortunate Richard". The main character leaves a smallish amount of money in his will, five lots of 100 <i><a href="/wiki/French_livre" title="French livre">livres</a></i>, to collect interest over one, two, three, four or five full centuries, with the resulting astronomical sums to be spent on impossibly elaborate utopian projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Franklin, who was 79 years old at the time, wrote thanking him for a great idea and telling him that he had decided to leave a bequest of 1,000 pounds each to his native Boston and his adopted Philadelphia. By 1990, more than $2,000,000 had accumulated in Franklin's Philadelphia trust, which had loaned the money to local residents. From 1940 to 1990, the money was used mostly for mortgage loans. When the trust came due, Philadelphia decided to spend it on scholarships for local high school students. Franklin's Boston trust fund accumulated almost $5,000,000 during that same time; at the end of its first 100 years a portion was allocated to help establish a <a href="/wiki/Vocational_school" title="Vocational school">trade school</a> that became the <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Institute_of_Technology" title="Benjamin Franklin Institute of Technology">Franklin Institute of Boston</a>, and the whole fund was later dedicated to supporting this institute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Franklin_on_U.S._postage">Franklin on U.S. postage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="686" data-file-width="546" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg/340px-Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_1861_Issue-1c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Issue of 1861</center></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="561" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg/190px-Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg/285px-Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg/380px-Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin2_1895_Issue-1c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Issue of 1895</center></div></div></div>
<p>Benjamin Franklin is a prominent figure in American history comparable to Washington, Jefferson and Lincoln, and as such he has been honored on U.S. postage stamps many times. The image of Franklin, the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a> of the United States, occurs on the face of U.S. postage more than any other notable American save that of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scotts_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scotts-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Franklin appeared on the first U.S. postage stamp <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin#Postmaster" title="Benjamin Franklin">(displayed above)</a> issued in 1847. From 1908 through 1923 the U.S. Post Office issued a series of postage stamps commonly referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington-Franklin_Issues" title="Washington-Franklin Issues">Washington-Franklin Issues</a> where, along with George Washington, Franklin was depicted many times over a 14-year period, the longest run of any one series in U.S. postal history. Along with the regular issue stamps Franklin however only appears on a few <a href="/wiki/File:Ben_Franklin_250th_1956_issue-3c.jpg" title="File:Ben Franklin 250th 1956 issue-3c.jpg">commemorative stamps</a>. Some of the finest portrayals of Franklin on record can be found on the engravings inscribed on the face of U.S. postage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scotts_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scotts-211">[211]</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg"><img alt="Benjamin Franklin 2-Big-Bens 1918 Issue" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1333" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg/350px-Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg/525px-Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg/700px-Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_2-Big-Bens_1918_Issue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Issue of 1918</center></div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bawdy_Ben">Bawdy Ben</span></h3>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Advice_to_a_Friend_on_Choosing_a_Mistress" title="Advice to a Friend on Choosing a Mistress">Advice to a Friend on Choosing a Mistress</a>" is a letter written by Benjamin Franklin, dated June 25, 1745, in which Franklin gives advice to a young man about channeling sexual urges. Due to its licentious nature, the letter was not published in collections of Franklin's papers during the nineteenth century. Federal court decisions from the mid-to-late twentieth century cited the document as a reason for overturning obscenity laws, using it to make a case against censorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exhibitions">Exhibitions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2012-07_ncc_04.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/2012-07_ncc_04.JPG/220px-2012-07_ncc_04.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/2012-07_ncc_04.JPG/330px-2012-07_ncc_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/2012-07_ncc_04.JPG/440px-2012-07_ncc_04.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2012-07_ncc_04.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Life-size bronze statue of Benjamin Franklin (seated) in the <a href="/wiki/National_Constitution_Center" title="National Constitution Center">National Constitution Center</a>, Philadelphia</div></div></div>
<p>"The Princess and the Patriot: <a href="/wiki/Yekaterina_Vorontsova-Dashkova" title="Yekaterina Vorontsova-Dashkova">Ekaterina Dashkova</a>, Benjamin Franklin and the Age of Enlightenment" exhibition opened in Philadelphia in February 2006 and ran through December 2006. Benjamin Franklin and Dashkova met only once, in Paris in 1781. Franklin was 75, and Dashkova was 37. Franklin invited Dashkova to become the first woman to join the American Philosophical Society; she was the only woman so honored for another 80 years. Later, Dashkova reciprocated by making him the first American member of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">Russian Academy of Sciences</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_and_things_named_after_Benjamin_Franklin">Places and things named after Benjamin Franklin</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_named_for_Benjamin_Franklin" title="List of places named for Benjamin Franklin">List of places named for Benjamin Franklin</a></div>
<p>As a founding father of the United States, Franklin's name has been attached to many things. Among these are:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/State_of_Franklin" title="State of Franklin">State of Franklin</a>, a short-lived independent state formed during the American Revolutionary War</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/County_(United_States)" title="County (United States)">Counties</a> in at least 16 U.S. states</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Institute_Awards" title="Franklin Institute Awards">Franklin Institute Awards</a> (presented by the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Institute" title="Franklin Institute">Franklin Institute</a>) for significant contributions in the fields of science and engineering.</li>
<li>Several major landmarks in and around <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, Pennsylvania, Franklin's longtime home, including:
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_and_Marshall_College" title="Franklin and Marshall College">Franklin and Marshall College</a> in nearby <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster, Pennsylvania">Lancaster</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Franklin_Field" title="Franklin Field">Franklin Field</a>, a <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a> field once home to the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Eagles" title="Philadelphia Eagles">Philadelphia Eagles</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> and the home field of the <a href="/wiki/Penn_Quakers_football" title="Penn Quakers football">University of Pennsylvania Quakers</a> since 1895</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Bridge" title="Benjamin Franklin Bridge">Benjamin Franklin Bridge</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a> between Philadelphia and <a href="/wiki/Camden,_New_Jersey" title="Camden, New Jersey">Camden, New Jersey</a></li></ul></li>
<li>Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Navy" title="US Navy">US Navy</a> ships have been named the <a href="/wiki/USS_Franklin" title="USS Franklin">USS <i>Franklin</i></a> or the <a href="/wiki/USS_Bonhomme_Richard" title="USS Bonhomme Richard">USS <i>Bonhomme Richard</i></a>, the latter being a French translation of his penname "Poor Richard". Two aircraft carriers, <a href="/wiki/USS_Franklin_(CV-13)" title="USS Franklin (CV-13)">USS <i>Franklin</i> (CV-13)</a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Bon_Homme_Richard_(CV-31)" title="USS Bon Homme Richard (CV-31)">USS <i>Bon Homme Richard</i> (CV-31)</a>, were simultaneously in commission and in operation during World War II, and Franklin therefore had the distinction of having two simultaneously operational US Navy warships named in his honor. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_ship_Franklin_(1797)" title="French ship Franklin (1797)">French ship <i>Franklin</i> (1797)</a> was also named in Franklin's honor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/CMA_CGM_Benjamin_Franklin" title="CMA CGM Benjamin Franklin">CMA CGM <i>Benjamin Franklin</i></a>, a Chinese-built French owned <a href="/wiki/Explorer-class_container_ship" title="Explorer-class container ship">Explorer-class container ship</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-calendar_group=Note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-calendar_group=Note_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Contemporary records, which used the Julian calendar and the <a href="/wiki/New_Year#Historical_Christian_new_year_dates" title="New Year">Annunciation Style</a> of enumerating years, recorded his birth as January 6, 1705. The provisions of the British <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">Calendar (New Style) Act 1750</a>, implemented in 1752, altered the official British dating method to the Gregorian calendar with the start of the year on January 1 (it had been March 25). These changes resulted in dates being moved forward 11 days, and for those between January 1 and March 25, an advance of one year. For a further explanation, see: <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">Old Style and New Style dates</a>.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-lightning_group=Note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-lightning_group=Note_141-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Portraits of Franklin at this time often contained an inscription, the best known being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anne-Robert-Jacques_Turgot,_Baron_de_Laune" title="Anne-Robert-Jacques Turgot, Baron de Laune">Turgot's</a> acclamation, "<i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Eripuit fulmen coelo sceptrumque tyrannis.</i>" (He snatched the lightning from the skies and the scepter from the tyrants.) Historian <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Christoph_Schlosser" title="Friedrich Christoph Schlosser">Friedrich Christoph Schlosser</a> remarked at the time, with ample hyperbole, that "Such was the number of portraits, busts and medallions of him in circulation before he left Paris, that he would have been recognized from them by any adult citizen in any part of the civilized world." – <img alt="Wikisource" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/12px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/18px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/> <cite class="citation encyclopaedia">Chisholm, Hugh, ed. (1911). "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/1911_Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica/Franklin,_Benjamin" title="wikisource:1911 Encyclopædia Britannica/Franklin, Benjamin">Franklin, Benjamin</a>". <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="Encyclopædia Britannica Eleventh Edition">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i> (11th ed.). <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_studies">Scholarly studies</span></h3>
<ul><li>Anderson, Douglas. <i>The Radical Enlightenments of Benjamin Franklin</i> (1997) – fresh look at the intellectual roots of Franklin</li>
<li>Buxbaum, M.H., ed. <i>Critical Essays on Benjamin Franklin</i> (1987)</li>
<li>Chaplin, Joyce. <i>The First Scientific American: Benjamin Franklin and the Pursuit of Genius.</i> (2007)</li>
<li>Cohen, I. Bernard. <i>Benjamin Franklin's Science</i> (1990) – Cohen, the leading specialist, has several books on Franklin's science</li>
<li>Conner, Paul W. <i>Poor Richard's Politicks</i> (1965) – analyzes Franklin's ideas in terms of the Enlightenment and republicanism</li>
<li>Dull, Jonathan. <i>Benjamin Franklin and the American Revolution</i> (2010)</li>
<li>Dull, Jonathan. <i>A Diplomatic History of the American Revolution</i> (1985)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Philip_Dray" title="Philip Dray">Dray, Philip</a>. <i>Stealing God's Thunder: Benjamin Franklin's Lightning Rod and the Invention of America.</i> (2005). 279 pp.</li>
<li>Ford, Paul Leicester. <i>The Many-Sided Franklin</i> (1899) <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=lU8j4QVPP_MC&amp;dq=intitle:The+intitle:Many-Sided+intitle:Franklin+inauthor:ford&amp;lr=&amp;as_drrb_is=q&amp;as_minm_is=0&amp;as_miny_is=&amp;as_maxm_is=0&amp;as_maxy_is=&amp;as_brr=0&amp;ei=FyIRTM33BoiIkgTx-oy4CQ" rel="nofollow">online edition</a> – collection of scholarly essays
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://cdl.library.cornell.edu/cgi-bin/moa/sgml/moa-idx?notisid=ABP2287-0057-169" rel="nofollow">"Franklin as Printer and Publisher"</a> in <i>The Century</i> (April 1899) v. 57 pp. 803–18.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://cdl.library.cornell.edu/cgi-bin/moa/sgml/moa-idx?notisid=ABP2287-0058-172" rel="nofollow">"Franklin as Scientist"</a> in <i>The Century</i> (September 1899) v.57 pp. 750–63. By Paul Leicester Ford.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://cdl.library.cornell.edu/cgi-bin/moa/sgml/moa-idx?notisid=ABP2287-0058-201" rel="nofollow">"Franklin as Politician and Diplomatist"</a> in <i>The Century</i> (October 1899) v. 57 pp. 881–99. By Paul Leicester Ford.</li></ul></li>
<li>Gleason, Philip. "Trouble in the Colonial Melting Pot." <i>Journal of American Ethnic History</i> 2000 20(1): 3–17.</li>
<li>Houston, Alan. <i>Benjamin Franklin and the Politics of Improvement</i> (2009)</li>
<li>Lemay, J. A. Leo, ed. <i>Reappraising Benjamin Franklin: A Bicentennial Perspective</i> (1993) – scholarly essays</li>
<li>Mathews, L. K. "Benjamin Franklin's Plans for a Colonial Union, 1750–1775." <i>American Political Science Review</i> 8 (August 1914): 393–412.</li>
<li><cite class="citation journal"><a href="/wiki/Drew_R._McCoy" title="Drew R. McCoy">McCoy, Drew R.</a> (1978). "Benjamin Franklin's Vision of a Republican Political Economy for America". <i>William and Mary Quarterly</i>. <b>35</b> (4): 607–28. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/1923207" rel="nofollow">1923207</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=William+and+Mary+Quarterly&amp;rft.atitle=Benjamin+Franklin%27s+Vision+of+a+Republican+Political+Economy+for+America&amp;rft.volume=35&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=607-28&amp;rft.date=1978&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F1923207&amp;rft.aulast=McCoy&amp;rft.aufirst=Drew+R.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABenjamin+Franklin"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></li>
<li>Merli, Frank J., and Theodore A. Wilson, eds. <i>Makers of American diplomacy, from Benjamin Franklin to Henry Kissinger</i> (1974) <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/makersofamerican00merl" rel="nofollow">online free</a></li>
<li>Newman, Simon P. "Benjamin Franklin and the Leather-Apron Men: The Politics of Class in Eighteenth-Century Philadelphia", <i>Journal of American Studies</i>, August 2009, Vol. 43#2 pp. 161–75; Franklin took pride in his working class origins and his printer's skills.</li>
<li>Olson, Lester C. <i>Benjamin Franklin's Vision of American Community: A Study in Rhetorical Iconology.</i> (2004). 323 pp.</li>
<li>Schiffer, Michael Brian. <i>Draw the Lightning Down: Benjamin Franklin and Electrical Technology in the Age of Enlightenment.</i> (2003). 383 pp.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.bartleby.com/225/index.html#6" rel="nofollow">Stuart Sherman "Franklin"</a> 1918 article on Franklin's writings.</li>
<li>Skemp, Sheila L. <i>Benjamin and William Franklin: Father and Son, Patriot and Loyalist</i> (1994) – Ben's son was a leading Loyalist</li>
<li>Sletcher, Michael. 'Domesticity: The Human Side of Benjamin Franklin', <i>Magazine of History</i>, XXI (2006).</li>
<li>Waldstreicher, David. <i>Runaway America: Benjamin Franklin, Slavery, and the American Revolution.</i> Hill and Wang, 2004. 315 pp.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kerry_S._Walters" title="Kerry S. Walters">Walters, Kerry S.</a> <i>Benjamin Franklin and His Gods.</i> (1999). 213 pp. Takes position midway between D H Lawrence's brutal 1930 denunciation of Franklin's religion as nothing more than a bourgeois commercialism tricked out in shallow utilitarian moralisms and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Owen_Aldridge" title="Owen Aldridge">Owen Aldridge</a>'s sympathetic 1967 treatment of the dynamism and protean character of Franklin's "polytheistic" religion.</li>
<li>York, Neil. "When Words Fail: William Pitt, Benjamin Franklin and the Imperial Crisis of 1766", <i>Parliamentary History</i>, October 2009, Vol. 28#3 pp. 341–74</li></ul>

<ul><li>Waldstreicher, David, ed. <i>A Companion to Benjamin Franklin</i> (2011), 25 essays by scholars emphasizing how historians have handled Franklin. <a class="external text" href="https://www.questia.com/library/120084135/a-companion-to-benjamin-franklin" rel="nofollow">online edition</a></li></ul>
